          "href": "http://www.travelsecurity.com/Page.aspx?pg=20354",
          "title": "<![CDATA[Sporadic Zika transmission]]>",
          "summary": "<![CDATA[Since 2013, sporadic transmission of Zika virus has been reported in the population or travellers in a number of countries / territories in Asia and the Pacific. These countries may have Zika virus in the mosquito population, which may occasionally infect people. There appears to be no outbreak or active transmission at present, and overall the risk of acquiring Zika appears low, however the situation may change as testing and surveillance is increased. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. Sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms of Zika are usually mild and self-limited, there is a risk of severe and irreversible birth defects in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing. The situation is continuing to evolve. Updates, Facts and FAQs are available at the Zika website]]>",
          "created": "2016-07-11T20:46:27Z",
          "modified": "2017-02-27T06:57:13Z",
          "categories": [
            {
              "primary": "Medical Alert ",
              "sub": " Disease Outbreak"
            }
          ],
          "long": 90,
          "lat": 24,
          "id": 947509,
          "version": 18,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Bangladesh; Cambodia; Easter Island </b> - Chile; <b>Cook Islands; French Polynesia (France); Indonesia; Laos; Maldives; Solomon Islands; Vanuatu; Myanmar; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Zika virus<br />\n</p>\n<p><p> Since 2013, sporadic transmission of Zika virus has been reported in the population or travellers in a number of countries / territories in Asia and the Pacific. These countries may have Zika virus in the mosquito population, which may occasionally infect people. There appears to be no outbreak or active transmission at present, and overall the risk of acquiring Zika appears low, however the situation may change as testing and surveillance is increased. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. Sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms of Zika are usually mild and self-limited, there is a risk of severe and irreversible birth defects in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing. The situation is continuing to evolve. Updates, Facts and FAQs are available at the <a href=\"https://pandemic.internationalsos.com/zika\" target=\"_blank\">Zika website</a></p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing. In addition to the infectious risks of any destination, it is important to consider the standard of available healthcare, and the availability of obstetric and neonatal specialist support (should it be required). </p>\n<p>For those who will travel, or are in areas where sporadic cases of Zika have been reported:</p>\n<ul> <li>Prevent infection by preventing mosquito bites.</li> <ul> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535.</li> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks).</li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire.</li> <li>Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room.</li> <li>Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.</li> <li>Consider using an insecticide-treated bed net.</li> </ul> <li>Prevent sexual transmission through the use of condoms (or abstinence).</li>\n</ul>\n<ul>\n</ul>\n<p>After travel:</p>\n<ul> <li>Pregnant women - Consult your doctor. You may be advised to be tested for Zika whether or not you develop symptoms. Your doctor may also arrange additional monitoring or specialist care. </li> <li>All travellers - Monitor your health for two weeks. If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor and advise them of your travel history. This is especially important if you are, or are trying to fall pregnant. </li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission - Anyone who has a pregnant partner should use condoms (applicable to males and females) or abstain for the duration of the pregnancy. For those whose partners are not pregnant, authorities differ in their recommendations for the duration for which precautions should continue. <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">The World Health Organization advises</a> that everyone should continue precautions for at least 6 months after return. <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/prevention/protect-yourself-during-sex.html\" target=\"_blank\">The United States CDC advises</a>: <strong>Men</strong> should continue precautions for at least 6 months; <strong>Women </strong>to continue precautions for at least 8 weeks. </li> <li>Consider delaying pregnancy - The World Health Organization <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> couples or women planning a pregnancy, who have returned from an area with Zika transmission, to wait at least 6 months. The United States CDC <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/pregnancy/women-and-their-partners.html\" target=\"_blank\">recommend</a> women trying to get pregnant wait at least 8 weeks and partners of women trying to get pregnant wait at least 6 months. </li> <li>Prevent transmission to local mosquitoes. If you are returning to an area that has mosquitoes which can transmit Zika, continue to prevent mosquito bites for 2 to 3 weeks (e.g. using insect repellent). This will reduce the risk of infecting local mosquitoes with Zika, and therefore reduce the risk of an outbreak.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>See \"Zika virus\" in the \"Health Threats\" section of the country guide of interest.</p>\n<p>Zika is a viral illness that is mostly spread via mosquito bites, although sexual transmission occurs. Four out of five people infected have no symptoms. When symptoms occur, they are usually mild - fever, rash, muscle and joint pains, and red eyes (conjunctivitis), similar to many other viral illnesses including flu, dengue and chikungunya. Most people recover fully in about a week. Occasionally neurological complications including \"Guillain–Barr syndrome\" (a potentially severe muscular weakness) occurs. Irreversible severe birth defects, including \"microcephaly\" (a small head with severe brain and developmental abnormalities) can occur in the babies of women infected during pregnancy. There is no specific treatment and no vaccine. Prevention is through avoidance of mosquito bites and sexual transmission.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"
        }

          "summary": "<![CDATA[Locally-acquired Zika infection has been reported from approximately 50 countries and territories in the Americas region. In some locations transmission is widespread and growing, in others, sporadic or declining. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. While less common, sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms are usually mild and self limited, severe and irreversible birth defects may occur in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women should consider delaying travel to Zika-affected areas. Those who are in affected areas should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission. After travel to the affected areas, ongoing precautions against sexual transmission are recommended. The situation is continuing to evolve. Updates, Facts and FAQs are available at the Zika website.]]>",

          "long": -63.05,
          "lat": 18.21667,
          "id": 947511,
          "version": 21,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Anguilla (UK); Antigua and Barbuda; Argentina; Aruba (Netherlands); Bahamas; Barbados; Belize; Bolivia; Bonaire (Netherlands), Saba </b> - Bonaire (Netherlands); <b>Brazil; British Virgin Islands (UK); Cayman Islands (UK); Colombia; Costa Rica; Cuba; Curacao (Netherlands); Dominica; Dominican Republic; Ecuador; El Salvador; French Guiana (France); Grenada; Guadeloupe (France); Guatemala; Guyana; Haiti; Honduras; Jamaica; Martinique (France); Mexico; Nicaragua; Panama; Paraguay; Peru; Puerto Rico (US); Saint-Barthélemy </b> - ; <b>St Kitts and Nevis; St Lucia; St Martin (France); St Vincent and the Grenadines; St Maarten (Netherlands); </b> - Bonaire (Netherlands),St Eustatius (Netherlands); <b>Suriname; Turks and Caicos Islands; Trinidad and Tobago; US Virgin Islands (US); Venezuela; Montserrat (UK); </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Zika virus<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Locally-acquired Zika infection has been reported from approximately 50 countries and territories in the Americas region. In some locations transmission is widespread and growing, in others, sporadic or declining. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. While less common, sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms are usually mild and self limited, severe and irreversible birth defects may occur in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women should consider delaying travel to Zika-affected areas. Those who are in affected areas should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission. After travel to the affected areas, ongoing precautions against sexual transmission are recommended. The situation is continuing to evolve. Updates, Facts and FAQs are available at the <a href=\"https://pandemic.internationalsos.com/zika\" target=\"_blank\">Zika website</a>.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing. In addition to the infectious risks of any destination, it is important to consider the standard of available healthcare, and the availability of obstetric and neonatal specialist support (should it be required).</p>\n<p>For those who will travel, or are in areas where cases of Zika have been reported:</p>\n<ul> <li>Prevent infection by preventing mosquito bites. </li> <ul> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535. </li> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks). </li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire.</li> <li>Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room. Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.</li> <li>Consider the use of an insecticide-treated bed net.</li> </ul> <li>Prevent sexual transmission through the use of condoms. </li>\n</ul>\n<ul>\n</ul>\n<p>After travel:</p>\n<ul> <li>Monitor your health for two weeks. If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor. </li> <li>Pregnant women - consult your doctor. You may be advised to be tested for Zika whether or not you develop symptoms. Your doctor may also arrange additional monitoring or specialist care. </li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission - Anyone who has a pregnant partner should use condoms (applicable to males and females) or abstain for the duration of the pregnancy. For those whose partners are not pregnant, authorities differ in their recommendations for the duration for which precautions should continue. <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">The World Health Organization advises</a> that <strong>everyone</strong> should continue precautions for at least 6 months after return. <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/prevention/protect-yourself-during-sex.html\" target=\"_blank\">The United States CDC advises</a>: <strong>Men </strong>should continue precautions for at least 6 months; <strong>Women</strong> should continue precautions for at least 8 weeks. </li> <li>Consider delaying pregnancy - The World Health Organization <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> couples or women planning a pregnancy, who have returned from an area with Zika transmission, to wait at least 6 months. The United States CDC <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/pregnancy/women-and-their-partners.html\" target=\"_blank\">recommend</a> women trying to get pregnant wait at least 8 weeks and partners of women trying to get pregnant wait at least 6 months. </li> <li>Continue to prevent mosquito bites for two to three weeks to reduce the risk of infecting local mosquitoes with Zika.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing. (In areas above 2000 metres elevation (6,500 feet), the risk of Zika infection through mosquito bites is very low.) In addition to the infectious risks of any destination, it is important to consider the standard of available healthcare, and the availability of obstetric and neonatal specialist support (should it be required). Sexual transmission of Zika virus is possible and is prevented through the use of condoms. Many health authorities including the <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/pregnancy/index.html\" target=\"_blank\">United States CDC</a> and the <a href=\"http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/statements/2016/2nd-emergency-committee-zika/en/\" target=\"_blank\">World Health Organization</a> advise pregnant women not to travel to areas with active transmission of Zika virus.</p>\n<p>What is Zika?</p>\n<p>Zika is a viral illness that is mostly spread via mosquito bites, although sexual transmission occurs. Four out of five people infected have no symptoms. When symptoms occur, they are usually mild - fever, rash, muscle and joint pains, and red eyes (conjunctivitis), similar to many other viral illnesses including flu, dengue and chikungunya. Most people recover fully in about a week. Occasionally neurological complications including \"Guillain-Barr syndrome\" (a potentially severe muscular weakness) occurs. Irreversible severe birth defects, including \"microcephaly\" (a small head with severe brain and developmental abnormalities) can occur in the babies of women infected during pregnancy. There is no specific treatment and no vaccine. Prevention is through avoidance of mosquito bites and sexual transmission.\n</p>\nFor more information:<br/>\n<ul> <li>See the International SOS Zika website.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "modified": "2017-03-07T00:09:16Z",
          "categories": [
            {
              "primary": "Travel",

          "long": 126.939358,

          "version": 2,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>South Korea; North Korea; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Diplomatic issue, Conflict<br />\n</p>\n<p>North Korea early on 6 March fired four ballistic missiles off its north-western coast into the Sea of Japan (East Sea). Reports indicate that three of the projectiles flew 625 miles (1,000km) before landing in Japan's Exclusive Economic Zone (EEZ). Members should expect further bellicose rhetoric and provocative acts by North Korea amid heightened regional tensions. However, such acts do not indicate an overall deterioration in the travel security environment and travel to South and North Korea can continue.</p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to South Korea and North Korea can continue. Monitor developments prior to and during your stay for any deterioration in bilateral relations and associated risks.</li> <li>Tour visits to the Demilitarised Zone (DMZ) can continue. Visits are strictly controlled and will be quickly halted in the event of a deteriorating security environment.</li> <li>Members in South Korea should seek itinerary-specific advice prior to travel to the disputed islands of Yeonpyeong, Baengnyeong, Daecheong, Socheong and Woo; follow any evacuation orders issued by the authorities.</li> <li>Members planning to travel to North Korea should obtain a brief prior to departure on the unique circumstances and practical difficulties. The importance of this brief is amplified by the severe limitations on receiving reliable, timely information in the country.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts to remain apprised of related developments. Contact your nearest Assistance Centre to seek itinerary- and profile-specific advice.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p>The launch comes amid an ongoing annual US-South Korean Key Resolve and Foal Eagle joint military exercises, which began on 1 March and will continue until late April. North Korea frequently conducts missile and nuclear tests around the annual military exercises and important anniversaries. The latest missile launch was the second such incident this year; North Korea fired a medium-range ballistic missile off its eastern coast on 12 February. Previous nuclear tests and missile launches have prompted the imposition of UN-led and bilateral international sanctions on North Korea, which, in turn, is liable to prompt further bellicose rhetoric from the North.<br/>\n<p>Further provocative acts are increasingly probable until the joint exercises conclude, and if the US goes ahead with its plans to deploy an anti-missile defence system in South Korea. Nonetheless, the latest developments should not be viewed as a prelude to a military confrontation between North Korea and the US, South Korea or Japan. Precedents also indicate that North Korea may carry out small-scale and localised acts of military provocation – including exchanges of artillery fire – along the DMZ and the disputed maritime border in the Yellow Sea (West Sea). However, such incidents are unlikely to target civilian areas on the mainland.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Dengue is consistently present in the island and outbreaks are also ongoing in other Pacific islands. It is spread by mosquitoes and can cause a range of symptoms. It has no particular treatment. Some people, especially those who have been infected before, get a more severe form that can lead to fatal complications. Although a vaccine has been developed, it is only available in a small number of countries and is not recommended for travellers. Prevent infection by avoiding mosquito bites.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following: </p>\n<ul> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks). </li> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535. </li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room. </li> <li>Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible. </li> <li>Seek medical attention if you develop a high fever, especially if you suffer \"rigors\" (shaking) or a rash. </li>\n</ul>\n<p>For more information: </p>\n<ul> <li>About dengue: see \"Dengue Fever\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides. </li> <li>About preventing mosquito bites: see the Travel Advice articles \"Preventing Mosquito Bites\" and \"Using Insect Repellents Safely\" on the International SOS Country Guides. </li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on American Samoa. </li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Symptoms include vomiting and profuse, watery diarrhoea which can lead to severe dehydration. Travellers staying in quality accommodation are at low risk. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites however sexual transmission also occurs. Although symptoms of Zika are usually mild and self-limited, severe and irreversible birth defects may occur in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women are advised to consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing and to discuss their risks with their doctor. Those who are in affected areas should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission. After travel, ongoing precautions against sexual transmission are recommended.</p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss the risks of Zika infection with their doctor and consider postponing travel. </p>\n<p>In addition, pregnant women should consider the standard of healthcare available, especially obstetric and neonatal specialist support; discuss the risks with your doctor.</p>\n<p>For those who will travel to, or are in Angola:</p>\n<ul> <li>Prevent infection by preventing mosquito bites: Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535. When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks). Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Consider using an insecticide-treated bed net. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room. Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.</li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission through the use of condoms (or abstinence).</li>\n</ul>\n<p>After travel:</p>\n<ul> <li>Pregnant women - consult your doctor. You may be advised to be tested for Zika whether or not you develop symptoms. Your doctor may also arrange additional monitoring or specialist care.</li> <li>All travellers - monitor your health for two weeks. If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor and advise them of your travel history. This is especially important if you are, or are trying to fall pregnant.</li> <li>If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor and advise them of your travel history. This is especially important if you are, or are trying to fall pregnant.</li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission - Anyone who has a pregnant partner should use condoms (applicable to males and females) or abstain for the duration of the pregnancy. For those whose partners are not pregnant, authorities differ in their recommendations for the duration for which precautions should continue. <a href=\"http://mailgun.internationalsos.com/c/eJxtT8FugzAM_Rq4BSUkJXDIoRQyVVpPlXZPg9dGg4DiMLZ9_cJulSZZz8_Ss9_zoAQw-55fXQQ7BzhHmN4goJu9YvlDSVbKkho7MCotq42tK3azvAJhGxikzUf1iHHJ-DErdapt24rtMRfOxzRZDAkD4LwGC5j4st5GZ01M9_fxx32Y1BC-VjOSGIzHyeHuTpYAn-B3YRLADk8hX42_r-YOCnzu1OlQ1rrmHREdpwkOLWl73iTWcyFYc-iPp0zQgCviFJc9HgT_F8OMOGNh5ykPaoJx9g4KM47JUdB_ZddvTP7nQYmnQOdOZbKthGzkiXak15oRwVhPjm2ZWC-anupOV7rOZJe_XK4XQEwPpD0ua8qa-hf7sYBy\" target=\"_blank\">The World Health Organization advises</a> that everyone should continue precautions for at least 6 months after return. <a href=\"http://mailgun.internationalsos.com/c/eJxtT0FuwyAQfI19wzIYB3zgYMe4itScIvVOzMZBxRABTpq-vrS3SJVWuzPS7M6sFhTwfClPJsHsAxwSrB8QovFO4PIqFFEtJRzDWWmmycyxZlgTpjhhrG3PpRXXlG5F0xdkyvV4PKpZz9Xi75l9m0-Vxy3AHVzKN_-Iz1YJPf0WItgL0lswbkERvqprWu1LlHfllk0tIMCVRuxbwifejIiOTZ1bO6BBNl1GsqEUd63s9wWtQ9xiXNOtMi5BcOrXWNnoYzX7tQxiBeudgUpZCy7r_5WdnjH7H7SgL4EOoyjYsKOsY_t6RHKaMKIYS9QPJCNJO1lP47SbeMHG8u14OkKM-YG81zBe447_AIMxd1I\" target=\"_blank\">The United States CDC advises</a>: <strong>Men</strong> should continue precautions for at least 8 weeks if they did not have symptoms, but for at least 6 months if they did; <strong>Women</strong> to continue precautions for at least 8 weeks.</li> <li>Consider delaying pregnancy - The World Health Organization <a href=\"http://mailgun.internationalsos.com/c/eJxtT8FugzAM_Rq4BSUkJXDIoRQyVVpPlXZPg9dGg4DiMLZ9_cJulSZZz8_Ss9_zoAQw-55fXQQ7BzhHmN4goJu9YvlDSVbKkho7MCotq42tK3azvAJhGxikzUf1iHHJ-DErdapt24rtMRfOxzRZDAkD4LwGC5j4st5GZ01M9_fxx32Y1BC-VjOSGIzHyeHuTpYAn-B3YRLADk8hX42_r-YOCnzu1OlQ1rrmHREdpwkOLWl73iTWcyFYc-iPp0zQgCviFJc9HgT_F8OMOGNh5ykPaoJx9g4KM47JUdB_ZddvTP7nQYmnQOdOZbKthGzkiXak15oRwVhPjm2ZWC-anupOV7rOZJe_XK4XQEwPpD0ua8qa-hf7sYBy\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> couples or women planning a pregnancy, who have returned from an area with Zika transmission, to wait at least 6 months.</li> <li>Prevent transmission to local mosquitoes. If you are returning to an area that has mosquitoes which can transmit Zika, continue to prevent mosquito bites for 2 to 3 weeks (e.g. using insect repellent). In December 2016, a French tourist was suspected of contracting Zika whilst in Angola, however tests were not conclusive. The second case, an Angolan who lives in Luanda, tested positive for Zika in January 2017. The Ministry of Health has increased their surveillance throughout the country.</p>\n<p>Zika is a viral disease, mostly transmitted to people by mosquito bites. Zika virus can be passed from an infected pregnant mother to her baby. Spread through sexual contact occurs. Transmission via blood transfusion has occurred. Most people who have Zika infections do not have any symptoms. If symptoms occur, they are similar to chikunguyna or dengue, and include fever, rash, joint and muscle pains or conjunctivitis (red eyes), and typically last up to a week. The virus can cause severe irreversible birth defects, such as \"microcephaly\" (a small head with developmental abnormalities), and serious neurological complications. There is no specific treatment available, and no vaccine to prevent the disease.</p>\n<p>Zika may have been established in the local mosquito population in parts of Angola, however the extent and intensity of spread is uncertain at this time.</p>\nFor more information:<br/>\n<ul> <li>See the <a href=\"https://pandemic.internationalsos.com/zika\" target=\"_blank\">International SOS Zika website</a>. </li> <li>See \"Zika virus\" in the Diseases and Prevention dropdown on International SOS Country Guides. </li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p>The situation is continuing to evolve. The transmission intensity is uncertain. An increase in cases is likely as screening and testing is increased.</p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of:</p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including Zika, who is most at risk (such as pregnant women), and prevention strategies.</li> <li>Who to contact for advice and treatment if they get sick during or after travel.</li> <li>How to access bite prevention resources including insect repellent and bed nets.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Consider:</p>\n<ul> <li>Providing a travel health kit, including insect repellent.</li> <li>Stipulating a medical pre-travel consultation.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Locally-acquired cases of dengue fever have been reported in Far North Queensland. Other viruses spread via mosquito bites, such as Ross River and Barmah Forest viruses, are increasing, particularly in parts of New South Wales, Victoria and Western Australia. Symptoms common to these diseases include fever, muscle and joint aches, headache and rash. Prevent infection by avoiding mosquito bites.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Prevent mosquito bites at all times:</p>\n<ul> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers as much of your skin as is practical (long sleeves, long pants, socks).</li> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535.</li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with insect screens.</li> <li>Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room.</li> <li>Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.;</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p>For more information:\n<ul> <li>See the International SOS country guides: \"Health Threats\" section for Australia | \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown Barmah Forest Disease, Dengue Fever and Ross River fever | \"Travel Advice\" articles Preventing Mosquito Bites and Using Insect Repellents Safely.</li> <li>See the International SOS country guide on Australia.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

The national security minister announced these and other security measures on 15 February, after a wave of gang-related crime led to 27 murders. If violence were to continue at this level, the homicide rate by the year's end would be nearly double that of 2016. The spike in homicides and the security force response underscore the persistent risks posed by crime in the Bahamas and the need for travellers to exercise precautions at all times.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Take sensible precautions to mitigate the risks of petty and street crime. Maintain a low profile. Dress as inconspicuously as possible and avoid displays of wealth. Carry limited amounts of cash and keep jewellery and other valuables out of sight, ideally in a hotel safe.</li> <li>Avoid travelling on foot after dark. If you are accosted by a criminal, assume that your assailant is armed; do nothing to resist or antagonise them.</li> <li>Understand the basic geography of your destination, and ensure you familiarise yourselves with key routes: avoid high-crime or low-income areas if possible.</li> <li>Expect, but do not be unduly disturbed by, a heightened military and police presence.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on the Bahamas for further developments.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Pickpocketing, purse-snatching, burglary and armed robbery are the most commonly reported crimes against foreigners in major cities in the Bahamas. Foreign travellers may be targeted because of their perceived wealth. Criminal incidents are regularly reported near major tourist hotels, especially at night. In the capital Nassau, the 'Over-the-Hill' district to the south of the city centre and isolated beaches remain particularly vulnerable to crime after dark. While there have been reports of an increase in violent crime, such incidents usually occur in low-income and underdeveloped areas. </p>\n<p>The Royal Bahamas Police Force (RBPF) has a reputation for efficiency and effectiveness. However, the deployment of military personnel indicates perceived inadequacies in the abilities of law enforcement officers to both defend themselves and quell the latest crime wave, as well as a lack of unified policy among Bahamian security institutions. </p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

The murder, which came a week after an Italian national was also killed in the capital <a href=\"https://goo.gl/zo9iIM\" target=\"_blank\">Dhaka</a>, underlines the threat of persistent militancy in the country. While travel to Bangladesh can continue, members should maintain a low profile, ensure due diligence in the selection of accommodation and observe proper journey management protocols. </p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to Bangladesh can continue with stringent measures; these include maintaining a low profile, varying day-to-day routines as far as possible, and maintaining heightened awareness at all times. </li> <li>Exercise vigilance and minimise time spent at potential targets for attack, including government buildings, including courts; foreign government and commercial interests; and venues where foreigners or the political elite are known to congregate, including the lobbies of business-class hotels.</li> <li>Ensure that due diligence of accommodation is conducted during the selection process, identifying the level of security in place, the location of the hotel and any supporting infrastructure.</li> <li>Comprehensive journey management planning is essential. At a minimum, pre-arrange for a trusted local colleague or hotel representative to meet you on arrival. A private vehicle and driver is recommended for all overland travel.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on Bangladesh for further developments and contact the nearest Assistance Centre for profile- and itinerary-specific advice.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The Middle East-based Islamic State (IS) had claimed credit for the killing of both the Japanese and Italian nationals, along with several other fatal attacks in the country since at least 2015. There is currently no known evidence that IS is providing funding, weapons or other material support to enhance the operational capabilities of local militants. However, transnational Islamist militant networks, including IS and al-Qaida, are believed to be operating through connections with local affiliates.</p>\n<p>The authorities have continued to issue denials on the existence of transnational militant networks in the country. Instead, established domestic militant groups such as the JMB, the Ansarullah Bangla Team (ABT) as well as the Ansar al-Islam (Al) are often blamed for carrying out attacks, driven in part by persistent political instability and the marginalisation of Islamic political parties.</p>\n<p>Local militant groups' activities are likely to continue to consist of small-scale attacks using knives/machetes, and easily acquired small arms, crude explosive devices. The most likely target for attack will remain civil society members and institutions seen as preventing these groups from Islamising Bangladeshi society. Government-affiliated buildings, rallies or events, as well as security force personnel and infrastructure are also key targets.</p>\n<p>The authorities have the capacity to co-ordinate and react to intelligence and prevent high profile or complex attacks. However, they are likely to find it increasingly difficult to detect and prevent one-off attacks against lower-security soft targets where foreigners congregate such as bars and restaurants, including those in the upscale Gulshan, Banani and Baridhara areas of Dhaka. Thus, the primary risk to foreigners remains incidental exposure to militant violence.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

The alert advised Japanese nationals in the country to take stringent security precautions and to inform the embassy if they receive similar messages. While the veracity of the text message has not been confirmed, militant violence is a persistent threat in the country and foreign travellers should continue to maintain heightened vigilance.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to Bangladesh can continue. However, ensure that you are fully briefed on the prevailing threats and necessary mitigating measures prior to travel. Members in Dhaka should maintain heightened situational awareness outside upscale areas of Banani, Baridhara and Gulshan. </li> <li>To mitigate risk, maintain a low profile, exercise vigilance and minimise time spent at potential targets for attack; these include: government buildings, including courts; foreign government and commercial interests; and venues where foreigners or the political elite are known to congregate, including the lobbies of business-class hotels. </li> <li>Although hotels are a potential target for Islamist terrorist attacks, we continue to advise business travellers to stay at international-standard hotels in Bangladesh. These hotels generally offer adequate security for travellers, as well as access to support services, such as multi-lingual staff, medical support and reliable drivers.</li> <li>Comprehensive journey management planning is essential. At a minimum, pre-arrange to be met on arrival by a trusted local colleague or hotel representative. A private vehicle and driver is recommended for all overland travel.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on Bangladesh for further developments.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Given the limited details available on the incident, it is premature to comment on the perpetrators or their motive. There is no indication at this stage of any specific threat against Japanese nationals in the country. </p>\n<p>Nonetheless, militancy poses a persistent threat to foreigners in Bangladesh, including those from countries that are part of the US-led campaign against Islamist extremist groups. Militants may also target civil society members and institutions seen as preventing the Islamisation of Bangladeshi society, including foreigners associated with missionary and non-Muslim religious charities. Such attacks may take the form of opportunistic and narrowly targeted killings of foreign nationals, as witnessed in late September and early October 2015. </p>\n<p>In addition, militants have shown increasing determination to directly target foreigners to maximise international attention, as seen in the 1-2 July attack, or to undermine the legitimacy of the ruling Awami League. Consequently, densely crowded locations - where even small explosions or gun attacks can result in high casualty figures - are increasingly vulnerable. Exposure to attacks in these locations, including marketplaces, pilgrimage sites, religious festivals, low-security commercial districts and transport hubs - represents the primary terrorism-related threat to foreigners in the country.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Most people with the disease had not been vaccinated and authorities have ramped up vaccination campaigns to prevent further spread of the disease. Areas most affected by the outbreak include Charleroi, Jodoigne, Namur and Verviers regions. In 2017, more than 70 cases have been reported to the Infectious Disease Surveillance Unit, ten times the usual number reported in an average year. Measles is caused by a highly contagious virus that spreads from person-to-person via infectious droplets. Prevention is through vaccination. </p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following: </p>\n<ul> <li>Ensure you are immune to measles before travelling. Lassa fever is a viral haemorrhagic illness. Transmission to humans mostly occurs through ingestion or inhalation of excretions from infected rodents, especially rats. The disease can also spread through direct contact with the body fluids of an infected person. Prevent infection through observing strict hygiene and avoiding contact with sick people.</p> Risk to the business traveller is low.<br/></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Follow strict hygiene procedures, including frequent handwashing.</li> <li>Select safe food and water.</li> <li>Avoid contact with rodents and sick people.</li> <li>Those at highest risk, working closely with infected patients, should discuss ribavirin prophylaxis with a specialist physician.</li> <li>If you do need to seek medical care for any reason, call International SOS and we will direct you to an appropriate facility.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Since mid-February, two cases of Lassa fever have been detected in the northern departments of Atakora and Borgou. Health authorities are acting to control the outbreak. Over 50 contacts have been identified and will undergo a 21-day observation period. Outbreaks of Lassa fever are common in West Africa, including Benin.\n</p>\n<br/> For More Information: <ul> <li>See \"Lassa Fever\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown.</li> <li>See Travel Advice Articles \"Selecting Safe Food and Water\" on the International SOS Country Guide.</li> <li>See the Travel Advice article \"Rodents spread diseases\" on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>About Benin: see the International SOS Country Guide.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul> </p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/march-2017/benin-lassa-fever-mar-2017.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

Members should follow all directives issued by the security forces and be aware that crime poses a significant risk notwithstanding the police's operational strength.</p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Normal travel to Vitoria and Vila Velha can resume with heightened situational awareness. Travel to the rest of Espirito Santo can continue with normal precautions.</li> <li>Maintain a low profile, avoiding trouble spots and implementing a thorough and robust journey management plan.</li> <li>Anticipate a heightened security force posture throughout the state, including increased patrols and checkpoints. Comply with all directives.</li> <li>If you are accosted by a criminal, assume that your assailant is armed; do nothing to resist or antagonise them.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on Brazil for further updates.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p>The work stoppage, which began on 3 February, precipitated a spike in lootings, robberies, shootings, and carjackings. However, the situation improved following the troop deployment, mostly in Vitoria and Vila Velha. Increased patrols and checkpoints can be expected in the coming weeks in the state.<br/>\n<br/>\nThe strike and related protests by family members of police officers were undertaken to denounce officers' working conditions and the alleged failure of the government to pay benefits or increase salaries. The work stoppage ended on 10 February following an agreement between police officers and the state government.</p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Brazil; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Yellow fever<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>The outbreak of yellow fever continues in Brazil. Initially affecting Minas Gerais State, cases have now been confirmed in the states of Espirito Santo and Sao Paulo. Suspected cases are also under investigation in the states of Bahia, Rio Grande do Norte and Tocantins. In addition, other areas of Brazil are considered at risk for yellow fever. Travellers visiting any area at risk of yellow fever should be vaccinated at least ten days prior to arrival. In addition, some travellers <strong> to and from</strong> Brazil are required to show proof of vaccination to gain entry.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>See your doctor well in advance of travel, and provide them with details of your intended destinations and route. Vaccination is both recommended and required for some travellers. The vaccination certificate becomes valid 10 days after the vaccine was administered. Vaccination requirements may change at any time.</p>\n<p>Vaccination is:</p>\n<ul> <li><strong>Recommended</strong> for everyone &gt; 9 months of age who will visit any risk areas within Brazil (see below for details). Travellers who have NOT been vaccinated are advised NOT to travel to outbreak areas.</li> <li><strong>Required</strong> for travellers arriving from: Angola, Democratic Republic of Congo (including people who have transited in these countries). Travellers arriving from the USA are NOT required to show proof of vaccination.</li> <li><strong>Required</strong> for some destinations after being in Brazil, as it is a country with a risk of yellow fever transmission. Some places require proof of vaccination if you enter directly from a risk country, others require proof if you arrive within 6 days of departure from a risk country (including having transited through a risk country). Always check the relevant country guide prior to your trip. Proof of vaccination is NOT required to enter the USA. </li>\n</ul>\n<p>Risk areas for yellow fever (see the <a href=\"https://groupmarketing.carto.com/viz/c9de008c-e6e7-11e6-a29e-0e3ebc282e83/embed_map\" target=\"_blank\">International SOS Map</a>)</p>\n<ul> <li>All of the states of: Acre, Amapa, Amazonas, Distrito Federal (including the capital city of Brasilia), Espirito Santo, Goias, Maranhao, Mato Grosso, Mato Grosso do Sul, Minas Gerais, Para, Rondonia, Roraima, Tocantins.</li> <li>Some areas within the states of: Bahia, Parana, Piaui, Rio Grande do Sul, Rio de Janeiro, Santa Catarina, and Sao Paulo state.</li> <li>The cities of Fortaleza, Recife, Rio de Janeiro, Salvador and Sao Paulo currently do NOT have a risk of yellow fever.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Prevent mosquito bites:</p>\n<ul> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD or IR3535. Wear protective clothing (long pants and sleeves, socks) as weather permits.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>For More Information:</p>\n<ul> <li>US Centers for Disease Control And Prevention <a href=\"https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/travel/yellow-fever-vaccination-clinics/search\" target=\"_blank\">Yellow Fever Vaccination Clinics</a></li> <li>See \"yellow fever\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown and the Travel Advice Articles \"Preventing Mosquito Bites\" and \"Using Insect Repellents Safely\" on the International SOS Country Guide.</li> <li>See the Brazilian <a href=\"http://www.blog.saude.gov.br/index.php/geral/52271-ministerio-da-saude-tira-duvidas-dos-internautas-sobre-a-febre-amarela\" target=\"_blank\">Ministry of Health yellow fever information portal</a> (in Portuguese).</li> <li>About Brazil: see the International SOS Country Guide.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Health officials have declared a public health emergency over a yellow fever outbreak. Over 350 cases have been confirmed, however around 1,400 suspected cases, and at least 224 deaths, have been reported. More cases are expected, and further geographic expansion may occur, due to people moving between states, yellow fever infection in monkeys and low vaccination coverage. The confirmed cases were identified in Minas Gerais, Espirito Santo and Sao Paulo. Minas Gerais remains the state with the highest activity.</p>\n<p>Bahia, Goias, Rio Grande do Norte and Tocantins have suspected cases under investigation. Areas of Bahia, Goias and Tocantins are known risk areas for yellow fever transmission, however to date Rio Grande do Norte has not been considered a state with risk of yellow fever transmission.</p>\n<p>Health authorities have increased distribution of yellow fever vaccine and implemented vector control activities in response the the outbreak.</p>\n<p>What is Yellow Fever?</p>\n<p>Yellow fever is a viral disease that occurs in parts of Africa and South America. It is transmitted by mosquitoes. Although some people infected with yellow fever only have a mild illness, others develop a serious toxic form of the disease. The symptoms range from a mild influenza-like syndrome to a severe hemorrhagic fever with liver failure. There is no specific treatment for the disease. Between 20%-50% of people who have a severe case of yellow fever die from the disease. Because yellow fever can spread easily, many countries require proof of an up-to-date yellow fever vaccination before they allow travellers to enter the nation. Some require all travellers to be vaccinated before entry, while others only require vaccination if you have recently travelled from an infected (or endemic) country.</p>\n<p>Yellow Fever Vaccination</p>\n<p>Vaccination is only available through designated yellow fever vaccination clinics. They will issue a signed and stamped International Certificate of Vaccination or Prophylaxis (ICVP) which becomes valid 10 days after the vaccination, and is valid for the lifetime of the person vaccinated.\nBooster doses are sometimes recommended after 10 years, and your doctor will make an individual recommendation based on your circumstances.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Authorities are concerned that the outbreak will grow, given the favourable factors for ongoing transmission of yellow fever. The recommendations and requirements for vaccination may change as more cases occur and new areas are affected. If locally-acquired cases are confirmed in Rio Grande do Norte, it is likely that travellers to that state will be recommended vaccination prior to travel.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of:</p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including yellow fever.</li> <li>Vaccination requirements.</li> <li>Bite prevention measures including insect repellent and bed nets.</li> <li>What to do if they get sick during and after travel.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/jan17/yellow-fever-brazil-31-jan-17.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

The outbreak is caused by the Seoul virus, a type of hantavirus. The viruses are carried by infected rodents and are excreted in their saliva, urine and faeces. Humans become infected through inhaling the virus, which is present in rodent excretions, when bitten, or when contaminated particles touch broken skin or enters through the eyes, nose and mouth. Rare cases of the disease can lead to serious kidney disease. The disease is not generally passed from person to person. Prevention is by avoiding contact with rodents and their environments, and maintaining strict hygiene. The risk to business travellers is low.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<ul> <li>Avoid direct contact with rodents, their faeces, saliva, urine or nests.</li> <li>Pay attention to general hygiene and food storage, always wash your hands thoroughly before eating.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Three human cases of Seoul hantavirus infection have been confirmed in Ontario, all of whom had contact with pet rats. A rat breeding facility in the province has also tested positive for the virus. Health authorities are closely monitoring the situations in Canada and in the United States where further cases are occurring.</p>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>About hantaviruses: See \"Hantaviruses\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown.</li> <li>See the Public Health Agency of Canada <a href=\"https://www.canada.ca/en/public-health/services/diseases/seoul-virus/prevention.html\" target=\"_blank\">website</a>.</li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on Canada.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/march-2017/canada-hantavirus-march-2017.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. Sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms of Zika are usually mild and self-limited, severe and irreversible birth defects may occur in the babies of women infected when pregnant. The situation is continuing to evolve. Updates, Facts and FAQs are available at the Zika website.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing. In addition to the infectious risks of any destination, it is important to consider the standard of available healthcare, and the availability of obstetric and neonatal specialist support (should it be required).</p>\n<ul> <li>Prevent infection by preventing mosquito bites.</li> <ul> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535.</li> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks).</li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire.</li> <li>Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room. Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.</li> </ul> <li>Prevent sexual transmission through the use of condoms.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Consider:</p>\n<ul> <li>The use of an insecticide-treated bed net.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>After travel:</p>\n<ul> <li>Monitor your health for two weeks. If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor.</li> <li>Pregnant women - consult your doctor. You may be advised to be tested for Zika whether or not you develop symptoms. Your doctor may also arrange additional monitoring or specialist care.</li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission - Anyone who has a pregnant partner should use condoms (applicable to males and females) or abstain for the duration of the pregnancy. For those whose partners are not pregnant, authorities differ in their recommendations for the duration for which precautions should continue. <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">The World Health Organization advises</a> that everyone should continue precautions for at least 6 months after return. <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/prevention/protect-yourself-during-sex.html\" target=\"_blank\">The United States CDC advises</a>: <strong>Men</strong> should continue precautions for at least 6 months; <strong>Women </strong>to continue precautions for at least 8 weeks. </li> <li>Consider delaying pregnancy - The World Health Organization <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> couples or women planning a pregnancy, who have returned from an area with Zika transmission, to wait at least 6 months. The United States CDC <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/pregnancy/women-and-their-partners.html\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> women trying to get pregnant wait at least 8 weeks and partners of women trying to get pregnant wait at least 6 months.</li> <li>Continue to prevent mosquito bites for two to three weeks to reduce the risk of infecting local mosquitoes with Zika.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Zika is a viral disease, mostly transmitted to people by mosquito bites. Zika virus can be passed from an infected pregnant mother to her baby. Spread through sexual contact (from men) occurs. Transmission via blood transfusion has occurred. Most people who have Zika infections do not have any symptoms. If symptoms occur, they are similar to chikunguyna or dengue, and include fever, rash, joint and muscle pains or conjunctivitis (red eyes), and typically last up to a week. The virus can cause severe irreversible birth defects, such as \"microcephaly\" (a small head with developmental abnormalities), and serious neurological complications. There is no specific treatment available, and no vaccine to prevent the disease.<br/>\n<br/>\nFor more information:<br/>\n<ul> <li>See the <a href=\"https://pandemic.internationalsos.com/zika\" target=\"_blank\">International SOS Zika website</a>. </li> <li>See \"Zika virus\" in the Diseases and Prevention dropdown on International SOS Country Guides. </li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul>\n</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>The virus is likely present in the local mosquito population and further human cases may occur.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of:</p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including Zika, who is most at risk (such as pregnant women), and prevention strategies. </li> <li>Who to contact for advice and treatment if they get sick during or after travel. </li> <li>How to access bite prevention resources including insect repellent and bed nets. </li>\n</ul>\n<p>Consider: <br/></p>\n<ul> <li>Providing a travel health kit, including insect repellent. </li> <li>Stipulating a medical pre-travel consultation. </li>\n</ul>\n</p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "long": 101.901875,
          "lat": 35.486703,
          "id": 951905,
          "version": 18,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>China; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Avian Influenza H7N9<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>There are further reports of human cases of bird flu, with some resulting in severe illness and death. Cases have been recorded in over a dozen provinces including Anhui, Fujian, Guangdong, Guizhou, Guangxi, Henan, Hunan, Hubei, Jiangsu, Jiangxi, Liaoning, Sichuan, Shandong, Shanghai and Zhejiang. Cases have been imported into Beijing and Yunnan. In addition, Macao, Hong Kong and Taiwan have reported imported cases from mainland China. The majority of people infected had direct contact with birds. Although there are several clusters where human to human spread cannot be ruled out, there is no indication bird flu is spreading readily between people. The overall risk of infection appears to be low. Prevent infection by paying attention to hygiene and avoiding contact with birds and their environment.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Do not go to live animal markets, poultry farms or pig farms.</li> <li>Avoid unnecessary contact with animals.</li> <li>Do not handle any birds, even if they appear well. Prevent children from doing so.</li> <li>Avoid touching any surfaces that may be contaminated by bird droppings. Do not swim in any body of water that is used by birds.</li> <li>Always maintain high levels of personal hygiene. Frequent hand washing is very important. Wash hands before and after food preparation and before eating.</li> <li>Thoroughly cook all poultry and poultry products, including eggs. Thoroughly wash any objects that touch the raw products and sanitise kitchen surfaces.</li> <li>Monitor the advice of local health authorities - they may screen travellers returning from China.</li> <li>Members in China who develop flu-like symptoms should call the Assistance Centre for advice before seeking care at a medical facility.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Since November 2016, over 420 human cases of bird flu have been confirmed, in a seasonal \"wave\" of infection. The vast majority have been caused by the A(H7N9) strain, with a few due to A(H5N6). At least one case of A(H9N2) was detected and has since recovered. Most of those infected had direct contact with poultry.</p>\n<p>Anhui, Guangdong, Jiangsu and Zhejiang have reported the most cases this season. Imported cases have been reported in Beijing and Yunnan. Macao and Hong Kong have had a number of cases which were imported from the mainland. Taiwan has reported one imported case in 2017 from Guangdong Province.</p>\n<p>The World Health Organization <a href=\"http://www.who.int/influenza/human_animal_interface/avian_influenza/riskassessment_AH7N9_201702/en/\" target=\"_blank\">report</a> three possible clusters where human to human transmission of the virus could not be ruled out.</p>\n<p>What is Bird Flu?<br/>\n<br/>\nBird flu, also known as avian influenza, refers to influenza A viruses that usually affect birds. Some strains can cause large outbreaks in poultry (farmed birds), causing flocks to suddenly die. As migratory wild birds can carry the virus without becoming ill, they can spread the viruses from area to area and even continent to continent.<br/>\n<br/>\nSome bird flu viruses infect humans. The H5N1 and H7N9 strains have infected hundreds of people, while a number of other bird flu viruses (including H5N6 and H10N8) are rarely occur in humans. The H9N2 strain is consistently found in poultry in China and causes infrequent human infection, usually after direct contact with poultry or contaminated environments.<br/>\n<br/>\nPeople get bird flu from direct contact with infected birds or their environment, although in some cases there was no such exposure (and it is unknown how these people were infected), and in some cases limited human-to-human transmission occurred. Symptoms develop usually 2-5 days after exposure to infection, although this may extend up to 17 days. Symptoms are of fever, cough, and some strains typically cause conjunctivitis (redness of the eyes). Some strains, including H7N9, H5N6 and H5N1 are capable of progressing to severe disease with breathing difficulty and lung failure, and the fatality rate can be around 30%. Treatment is with antiviral medication.</p>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See \"Avian Influenza H7N9\" and \"Avian Influenza H5N1\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides</li> <li>See the Traveller Advice and Avian Flu pages on the <a href=\"http://pandemic.internationalsos.com/\" target=\"_blank\">International SOS Pandemic Information Website</a></li> <li>Contact the Beijing, China Assistance Centre: +86 (0)10 6462 9100 or any International SOS assistance centre</li>\n</ul>\n<br/>\n</p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Bird flu viruses are consistently present in birds in many areas of China. More sporadic cases are likely to be reported in people who have direct contact with live poultry, and the seasonal increase is expected to continue. Small clusters of human to human transmission may be recorded in family members and others who are in close contact with symptomatic people, however the risk to the general traveller who avoids contact with live poultry and their environment is low.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they: </p>\n<ul> <li>Are aware of regional health threats including avian influenza.</li> <li>Use personal hygiene measures, including frequent hand washing.</li> <li>Consider taking a travel health kit, including hand sanitiser.</li> <li>Know what to do if they get sick during and after travel.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/february-2017/china_h7n9_5feb2017.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

All of the attackers were subsequently shot dead by the police. While the perpetrators and motive have not been identified, the incident is likely linked to the persistent ethnic-Uighur militancy in Xinjiang. Members travelling to the region should remain alert to their surroundings.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to Xinjiang can continue. However, members should be locally hosted and should ensure they have comprehensive journey-management plans in place.</li> <li>Remain alert to your surroundings and minimise time spent at potential targets for attack: these include government and security force buildings, public transport hubs and busy unsecured areas such as markets. Report any suspicious activity or suspect packages to the authorities.</li> <li>Carry identification to ease passage through security checkpoints. Nonetheless, ethnic tensions continue to run high in Xinjiang amid a large security force presence, particularly in the regional capital Urumqi; the region is rated as a MEDIUM travel risk destination, in contrast to the country's overall LOW travel risk rating. Violence is most common in the remote south-west prefectures of Hotan, Aksu (Akesu) and Kashgar (Kashi), where the population is predominantly Uighur and resentment towards the authorities is more pronounced. </p>\n<p>Individuals with little training or experience, who operate outside the formal structure of a group and have various motivations, are the principal perpetrators of attacks in Xinjiang; they have limited capability to carry out large attacks outside the region. Most such low-level violence focuses on symbols of government authority, or police or law enforcement units and assets. Incidents typically involve knives and explosives, and occur in public transport hubs and busy unsecured areas such as markets. </p>\n<p>Recent attacks include: </p>\n<ul> <li>28 December 2016: Three attackers drove a vehicle into a government building, set off a bomb and attacked people with knives in Moyu county of Hotan, killing two people and injuring three.</li> <li>10 September 2016: A police officer was killed and nine other people injured when an explosive device was detonated during a police raid of a militant hideout in Hotan.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Malaria is not a known risk in Cuba. The disease is spread through mosquito bites and can be fatal unless promptly treated. Chemoprophylaxis and mosquito bite avoidance help prevent malaria.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following to prevent mosquito bites:<br/>\nWhile outdoors:</p>\n<ul> <li>Wear clothing that covers most of the body (long sleeves and long pants) as practical.</li> <li>Use an effective insect repellent, such as one that contains DEET. Follow the manufacturer's instructions, and reapply after swimming or excessive sweating.</li>\n</ul>\nWhile indoors or asleep:<br/>\n<ul> <li>Choose accommodation that is air-conditioned, or has windows and doors with effective insect screens.</li> <li>Sleep under a mosquito bed net.</li> <li>Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room.</li>\n<li>If you go to the affected area and develop a fever with flu-like symptoms, seek medical attention promptly. They have been hospitalised in Cienfuegos for treatment. Authorities have stepped up measures to control mosquito-breeding in the affected area.</p>\n<p>Cuba was declared free of malaria by the World Health Organisation in 1973.</p>\n<p>What is Malaria?</p>\n<p>Malaria is a serious disease caused by one of five species of the Plasmodium parasite. It is transmitted by mosquitoes that bite at night, during the hours between dusk and dawn. Once a patient has been exposed to malaria, it can take as little as seven days or as long as one year to develop symptoms. These can be episodic and may include fever, chills, headache, muscle aches, fatigue, vomiting, diarrhoea and jaundice. If not promptly treated, malaria caused by Plasmodium falciparum can be rapidly fatal.</p>\n<p>For more information:</p> <ul> <li>See \"Malaria\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on International SOS Country Guides</li> <li>See \"Preventing Mosquito Bites\" and \"Using Insect Repellents Safely\" in the \"Travel Advice\" dropdown on International SOS Country Guides</li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on Cuba</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>The situation is evolving. </p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of:</p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including mosquito-borne diseases, who is most at risk and prevention strategies.</li> <li>Who to contact for advice and treatment if they get sick during or after travel.</li> <li>How to access bite prevention resources including insect repellents.</li>\n</ul>\nConsider:<br/>\n<ul> <li>Providing a travel health kit, including insect repellent.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/jan17/cubamalaria.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Dominican Republic; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Leptospirosis<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>An increase in cases of leptospirosis continues to be reported in 2017, especially in the north. Leptospirosis is always present in the Dominican Republic but the country experiences surges after storms and during the rainy season. The bacterial disease is mainly spread through contaminated water entering the body through the skin or mucous membranes (eyes, nose, or mouth). Symptoms are often mild and flu-like, but the infection can be severe. Untreated, it can cause organ failure which can be fatal. Antibiotics are used to treat the disease. Prevention is through avoiding exposure and paying attention to hygiene.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<ul> <li>Wash your hands with soap regularly and wash any skin cuts/abrasions thoroughly. </li> <li>Avoid contact with standing water, or floodwater, which could be contaminated. </li>\n</ul>\nIf contact is unavoidable:\n<ul> <li>wear protective clothing such as thigh-high waterproof boots and cover all cuts. Wash thoroughly afterwards. </li> <li>Speak to your doctor about using the antibiotic doxycycline to prevent infection.</li>\n</ul>\nFor further information:<br/>\n<ul> <li>About Leptospirosis: See the \"Leptospirosis\" article in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>About Dominican Republic: see the International SOS Country Guide.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Egypt; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Avian Influenza H5N1<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Human cases of H5N1 bird flu have been detected in Faiyum and Minya governorates. H5N1 infections are serious and can be fatal. Early treatment is critical. Most infections occur in people who have direct contact with poultry. There is no suspicion of the disease spreading in the general community. Overall, the risk of infection to travellers is low in those who avoid direct contact with birds (whether live or dead) and their droppings. Ensure all poultry for consumption is thoroughly cooked.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<ul> <li>Avoid farms and \"wet markets\", where live animals may be present.</li> <li>Do not touch birds, even if they appear healthy. Ensure all poultry and eggs are thoroughly cooked.</li> <li>Do not eat raw products, such as raw duck blood.</li> <li>Wash your hands before and after preparing food, and before eating.</li> <li>Do not swim in any body of water that is used by birds.</li> <li>Consider having an influenza vaccination. Although it will not protect you against bird flu, it reduces your risk of a \"co-infection\" with human influenza. While in an affected area, monitor your health.</li> <li>If you develop flu-like symptoms (fever, cough, and fatigue) and have had exposure to birds, seek medical attention.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The first cases of human infection with H5N1 bird flu for 2017 have been detected. Faiyum and Minya governorates are affected and at least one fatality has been recorded. Bird flu is consistently present in birds in Egypt, with frequent outbreaks in poultry and sporadic human cases. There is an ongoing risk to people in direct contact with live poultry.</p>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See \"Avian Influenza H5N1\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>See the Traveller Advice and Avian Flu pages on the International SOS Pandemic Information Website.</li> <li>About Egypt: see the International SOS Country Guide.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul>\n<ul>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/february-2017/egypt-h5n1-2017.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

All four divisions have been affected with the Western Division reporting the highest number of cases. Dengue outbreaks are also ongoing in other Pacific islands. The disease is consistently present in Fiji. Dengue is spread by mosquitoes and can cause a range of symptoms. It has no particular treatment. Some people, especially those who have been infected before, get a more severe form that can lead to fatal complications. Although a vaccine has been developed, it is only available in a small number of countries and is not recommended for travellers. Prevent infection by avoiding mosquito bites.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:\n</p>\n<ul> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks).</li> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535.</li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room.</li> <li>Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.</li> <li>Seek medical attention if you develop a high fever, especially if you suffer \"rigors\" (shaking) or a rash.</li>\n</ul>\nFor more information: <ul> <li>About dengue: see \"Dengue Fever\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides. </li> <li>About preventing mosquito bites: see the Travel Advice articles \"Preventing Mosquito Bites\" and \"Using Insect Repellents Safely\" on the International SOS Country Guides. </li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on Fiji. </li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul>\n</p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Fifty-one people were arrested and additional police deployed to the town.</p>\n<p>Such developments highlight the remaining tensions after Jammeh (in office 1994-2017) stepped down under strong international pressure and went into exile. This underlines the low, but continued risk of attacks by disgruntled Jammeh loyalists. </p>\n<p>The relaxation of restrictions on public gatherings and increased expectations of political reforms could see political unrest become a more regular feature of the future travel security environment.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Barrow's official inauguration in Gambia on 18 February followed a lengthy political crisis triggered by former president Yahya Jammeh's refusal to concede defeat in the December 2016 presidential election. Barrow, who took an earlier oath of office in Senegal in January, is widely popular. However, his new administration faces several challenges, including the weakness of government institutions and the political inexperience of cabinet members after Jammeh's lengthy reign. Concerns will mainly centre on disgruntled military personnel, with any further high-profile reshuffles raising the risk of mutiny or coup attempts in the short-to-medium term. </p>\n<p>Legislative elections are scheduled for <strong>6 April</strong>, and further resistance by Jammeh supporters to the recent change in government is possible ahead of this. Chemicals used to make explosive devices and electronic components required for remote ignition were found in the suspect's apartment. On 9 February, a 22-year-old Nigerian national and a 27-year-old Algerian national were taken into custody on suspicion of plotting terrorist activity, following raids targeting 12 locations, predominantly in the city of Goettingen. Weapons, ammunition and a machete were found during the operation; however, no concrete plans of an attack were discovered at any of the locations.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Germany remains on high alert following a fatal truck-ramming attack in the capital Berlin on 19 December 2016, in which 12 people were killed and 56 others injured. Measles is caused by a highly contagious virus that spreads from person-to-person via infected droplets. Outbreaks occur frequently in under-vaccinated populations. Common symptoms include fever, cough and a characteristic rash. The illness can result in serious complications, particularly when very young children, the elderly or pregnant women are infected. All travellers should ensure they are fully immunised against measles.</p>\n</p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p>Do the following:\n<ul> <li>Ensure you are immune to measles before travelling. Get vaccinated if you haven't had measles in the past or if you haven't had two doses of measles vaccine.</li> <li>Keep young children and other people who are not immune to measles away from outbreak areas.</li> <li>If you are unsure about your immunity and may have been exposed, or if you develop symptoms, seek medical attention. Call before visiting in person, so staff can protect themselves and other patients from this very contagious virus.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Measles cases have been increasing since the start of the year, with more than 1,500 diagnosed cases and at least two deaths reported. Around fourteen prefectures are affected, mostly around the capital city Conakry and in the regions of Bok , Kankan, Kindia and Nz r kor . About thirteen neighbouring prefectures have also been placed on alert. Health authorities are planning to vaccinate over 3 million children to curb the outbreak.</p>\n<p>For more information:\n</p>\n<ul> <li>About measles: See \"measles\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on Guinea.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/march-2017/guinea-measles-3-mar-2017.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

          "title": "<![CDATA[Zika in Bijagos archipelago]]>",
          "summary": "<![CDATA[The Bijagos archipelago continues to report new cases as local transmission gets established in the region. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. Sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms are usually mild and self-limited, complications can occur. Pregnant women are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel. Those who are in Guinea-Bissau should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission.
