          "created": "2016-07-02T07:13:55Z",
          "modified": "2017-02-27T08:01:36Z",
          "categories": [
            {
              "primary": "Medical Alert ",
              "sub": " Disease Outbreak"
            }
          ],
          "long": -15.83333,
          "lat": 11.28333,
          "id": 947283,
          "version": 17,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Guinea-Bissau; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Zika virus<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>The Bijagos archipelago continues to report new cases as local transmission gets established in the region. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. Sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms are usually mild and self-limited, complications can occur. Pregnant women are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel. Those who are in Guinea-Bissau should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission. After travel to the country, ongoing precautions against sexual transmission are recommended.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel. Many health authorities advise pregnant women not to travel to areas with active transmission of Zika virus. In addition to the infectious risks of Guinea-Bissau, it is important to consider the standard of available healthcare, and the availability of obstetric and neonatal specialist support (should it be required).</p>\n<p>For those who will travel to, or are in Guinea-Bissau:</p>\n<ul> <li>Prevent infection by preventing mosquito bites: Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535. When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks). Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Consider using an insecticide-treated bed net. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room. Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.</li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission through the use of condoms (or abstinence).</li>\n</ul>\n<p>After travel:</p>\n<ul> <li>Pregnant women - consult your doctor. You may be advised to be tested whether or not you develop symptoms. Your doctor may also arrange additional monitoring or specialist care.</li> <li>All travellers - monitor your health for two weeks. If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor and advise them of your travel history. This is especially important if you are, or are trying to fall pregnant. </li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission - Anyone who has a pregnant partner should use condoms (applicable to males and females) or abstain for the duration of the pregnancy. For those whose partners are not pregnant, authorities differ in their recommendations for the duration for which precautions should continue. <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">The World Health Organization advises</a> that everyone should continue precautions for at least 6 months after return. <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/prevention/protect-yourself-during-sex.html\" target=\"_blank\">The United States CDC advises</a>: <strong>Men</strong> should continue precautions for at least 6 months; <strong>Women </strong>to continue precautions for at least 8 weeks. </li> <li>Consider delaying pregnancy - The World Health Organization <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> couples or women planning a pregnancy, who have returned from an area with Zika transmission, to wait at least 6 months. The United States CDC <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/pregnancy/women-and-their-partners.html\" target=\"_blank\">recommends </a>women trying to get pregnant wait at least 8 weeks and partners of women trying to get pregnant wait at least 6 months.</li> <li>Continue to prevent mosquito bites for two to three weeks to reduce the risk of infecting local mosquitoes with Zika.</li>\n</ul>\n<p><br/>\n</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The first cases were identified on the island of Bubaque in July 2016. A subsequent investigation by health authorities in the Bijagos archipelago has confirmed at least seven more cases in August, as well as finding Zika virus in local mosquitoes. </p>\n<p>Cases of microcephaly have been reported from Oio and Gabu since April, however whether these are related to Zika virus has not yet been determined.</p>\n<p>Zika is a viral disease, mostly transmitted to people by mosquito bites. Zika virus can be passed from an infected pregnant mother to her baby. Spread through sexual contact (from men) occurs. Transmission via blood transfusion has occurred. Most people who have Zika infections do not have any symptoms. If symptoms occur, they are similar to chikunguyna or dengue, and include fever, rash, joint and muscle pains or conjunctivitis (red eyes), and typically last up to a week. The virus can cause severe irreversible birth defects, such as \"microcephaly\" (a small head with developmental abnormalities), and serious neurological complications. There is no specific treatment available, and no vaccine to prevent the disease.</p>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See the <a href=\"https://pandemic.internationalsos.com/zika\" target=\"_blank\">International SOS Zika website</a>.</li> <li>See \"Zika virus\" in the Diseases and Prevention dropdown on International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>The virus is likely present in the local mosquito population and further human cases may occur.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of:</p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including Zika, who is most at risk (such as pregnant women), and prevention strategies. </li> <li>Who to contact for advice and treatment if they get sick during or after travel. </li> <li>How to access bite prevention resources including insect repellent and bed nets.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Consider:\n</p>\n<ul> <li>Providing a travel health kit, including insect repellent. </li> <li>Stipulating a medical pre-travel consultation.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/july-2016/zika-guinea-bissau.jpg\"/></p>]]>"
        }

The festival will be celebrated on <strong>13 March</strong> throughout the country, particularly in the northern and western states, including the capital Delhi. Members are advised to pre-arrange transport options and reconfirm bookings due to limited transport services during the festive period. Travellers should also exercise sensible safety precautions and have situational awareness during the festival. <br/></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Allow additional time to complete journeys during the Holi festival period, pre-arrange transport and reconfirm them a few hours before the journey time as a precaution on the day.</li> <li>Due to the nature of the celebrations, members may prefer to remain indoors or, where practical, avoid lively crowds to avoid being targeted by revellers and take sensible security precautions against the risk of petty crime, particularly pickpocketing and bag-snatching. </li> <li>There is a credible risk of attack by militant separatist, Islamist extremist and communal groups. Potential targets include public transport; religious sites; busy, unsecured areas, such as marketplaces; government and military buildings and personnel; and venues where the Indian social elite and Westerners are known to congregate. Remain alert to your surroundings and report any suspicious activity or suspect packages to the police.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on India for further updates.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Revellers commonly throw coloured water and gulal (brightly coloured powder) at pedestrians as part of Holi celebrations. Moreover, incidents of drink-driving and rash driving, sexual harassment (locally known as 'eve-teasing') or applying gulal, and other forms of boisterous behaviour, are common during the festival. </p>\n<p><em>Travel disruption</em></p>\n<p>Public transport services in most cities will be limited on the day. There will also be a shortage of rental cars and taxis, including radio cabs. </p>\n<p><em>Terrorism</em></p>\n<p>Festival periods in the country are known flashpoints for terrorist groups due to large crowds and unrestricted public gatherings. In 2013, the authorities foiled a suspected plot to carry out attacks in Delhi during Holi, following the arrest of a Hizbul Mujahideen militant in Gorakhpur (Uttar Pradesh state), a few days before the festival. As such, increased security measures are likely at major public areas, including markets, tourist spots, religious sites and transport hubs, during this period. These measures could result in delays, particularly at transport hubs, where security checks are likely to be more elaborate than usual. </p>\n<p><em>Communal Unrest</em></p>\nThere is the potential for communal unrest during major festivals like Holi. However, this is likely to occur in smaller towns and villages, and in low-income neighbourhoods of major cities. <br/></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

While most rallies have a localised impact, those held in major cities, such as the state capitals Imphal (Manipur) and Lucknow (Uttar Pradesh), typically attract thousands of attendees and cause significant traffic disruption. Major political parties' offices and public spaces such as parks, fields, stadiums and convention centres are usually popular gathering locations. Heightened security measures, including checkpoints and spot-checks, have already been imposed due to an economic blockade in Manipur since December 2016; these are expected to be further tightened in the coming days. </p>\n<p><em>Communal violence</em></p>\n<p>There are additional risks from communal unrest between local Hindus and Muslims in Uttar Pradesh, where violence is more common during election periods. Although related attacks target rival groups and mostly occur in rural areas, these can involve clashes in public places using sharp weapons, such as swords, and small arms. This would pose credible incidental risks to bystanders.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Long queues at banks, ATMs and post offices have reduced, as new 500 and 2,000 (US$30) rupee notes have become more readily available. While the likelihood of related demonstrations has therefore diminished, members should continue to monitor developments and avoid any potential lingering protests, particularly before the country's budget, which is currently being debated, is passed by 1 April.</p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Refer to the websites of the <a href=\"http://finmin.nic.in/\" target=\"_blank\">Ministry of Finance</a> and the <a href=\"https://rbi.org.in/home.aspx\" target=\"_blank\">Reserve Bank of India</a> (RBI) for up-to-date information on the demonetisation process.</li> <li>Where possible, opt for online payment or non-cash financial transactions to minimise inconvenience.</li> <li>Liaise with local contacts for updates on any potential protests and avoid all such gatherings as a routine precaution.</li> <li>Monitor our travel security alerts on India for further updates.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p>The move by the ruling Bharatiya Janata Party-led coalition is part of a crackdown on corruption and tax evasion, as well as an attempt to counter the increasing number of counterfeit large-denomination notes, which are used by terrorist groups operating in the country.<br/>\n<p>Some key information about the currency replacement process is stated below; further details can be found at the Ministry of Finance and RBI websites:</p>\n<ul> <li>All non-cash financial transactions continue to operate as normal.</li> <li>Indian citizens and non-resident Indian citizens who were abroad during the period between 9 November and 30 December 2016 will be able to exchange and deposit old notes at RBI offices until 31 March and 30 June respectively.</li> <li>Weekly withdrawals of cash from ATMs have been limited to 24,000 rupees (US$350) per transaction for savings account holders, and will now be raised to 50,000 rupees (US$746) from 20 February. From 13 March, no cash withdrawal limit will be imposed. There is no withdrawal limit for current or overdraft account holders now.</li>\n</ul>\n<em>Inconvenience</em>\n<p>The demonetisation of the 500 and 1000 rupee notes primarily affected low-value commercial transactions, with the impact being most significant in smaller urban centres and rural areas where most shops usually require cash. Therefore, any noticeable effects from further easing of the cash shortage, including shortening queues in ATMs, will be felt in major urban centres first, before trickling to other parts of the country. Reports indicate that the authorities have said cash supply will return to normal levels by early April.</p>\n<p><em>Protests</em></p>\n<p>Many opposition groups throughout the country have demonstrated against the demonetisation process, criticising the drawn-out impact, especially to traders, farmers, retailers and people in remote areas who conduct transactions and receive payments in cash. Despite the easing of the shortage, further protests are still likely, though in lesser scale than previous protests. Such protests are likely to ease by early April.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Some cases have been severe, and fatalities have been documented. Many of those infected have tested positive for influenza A(H1N1)pdm09, known earlier as “swine flu”. This strain of flu now circulates as a seasonal strain, and current influenza vaccination provides protection. Flu is a viral illness, causing a sudden fever, cough, headache, weakness and muscular pains. Most cases are mild although some can be severe or even fatal. Pregnant women, adults over 65 and young children are at higher risk of severe illness. Prevention is through vaccination and attention to hygiene.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p>Do the following: <ul> <li>Get a seasonal flu vaccine each year.</li> <li>Pay close attention to hygiene.</li> <li>Avoid people who are obviously sick.</li> <li>If symptoms develop - seek medical attention. People in higher risk groups should seek medical attention promptly, while others should seek medical attention if symptoms are more than mild or are progressive, as treatment with antiviral medication is most effective when started early.</li>\n</ul>\nFor more information:<br/>\n<ul> <li>About Influenza: see the Disease &amp; Prevention article \"Influenza\" on the International SOS Country Guides</li> <li>About India: see the International SOS Country Guide</li> <li>See the International SOS Pandemic Preparedness website</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul>\n<br/> </p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/jan17/india_flu_activity2.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

However, there has been no corroboration of a media report on 18 February stating this, and the veracity of the threat is unclear. While the report is not therefore indicative of an increased threat of attacks against foreign companies in India, it does serve as a reminder of the persistent risk of terrorism across the country. Members should exercise caution at all times and report any suspicious activity or suspect packages to the authorities.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to India, including to and within major cities, can continue as normal. </li> <li>Remain alert to your surroundings at all times and report any suspicious activity or suspect packages to the authorities.</li> <li>Minimise time around potential targets in crowded and unsecured places, such as marketplaces, commercial centres and public transport hubs.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on India for further updates.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>JeM has previously voiced resentment against Western organisations and corporations in South Asia. While the group has shown the capability and intent to carry out attacks in the country in the past, the latest rhetoric does not highlight any increased threat to foreign nationals or interests in India. </p>\n<p>JeM's actions in recent years have been minor and mainly concentrated in the Kashmir region, primarily targeting government and security force infrastructure and personnel. Any related violence is usually small in scale, involving the use of homemade devices or readily available weapons. As such, there is a persistent threat of militant attacks in India, where several regional and domestic militant groups, such as the Pakistan-based Lashkar-e-Toiba (LeT) and the domestic Indian Mujahideen (IM), retain the intent and capability to conduct attacks.</p>\n<p>Crowded marketplaces and public transport, including the rapidly expanding metro services in several cities, remain prime militant targets because of their relatively lower levels of security. Places of worship are another key focus for militants seeking to foment communal tensions. While government and security buildings remain potential targets, and there are periodic official alerts indicating threats to aviation facilities and hotels, such facilities' heightened security measures are likely to provide some deterrence to militants.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

The counter-terrorism operation ‘Tinombola', which began in January 2016, has been extended until April. There is a persistent threat posed by extremist groups in Indonesia, some of which have ties with, and share the ideology of, transnational organisations. While normal travel can continue to Central Sulawesi, members should be fully briefed on the prevailing threats and necessary mitigating measures prior to travel.</p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to Poso and other parts of Central Sulawesi can continue. Overland travel in these areas should only be conducted with reliable logistics operators who are fully aware of the specific locations of the ongoing operation, and the latest military restrictions.</li> <li>Maintain a low profile and minimise time spent in the vicinity of police/security force facilities and assets, as well as buildings associated with religious minorities, including Christians and the Ahmadiya sect.</li> <li>Follow all security force directives and carry relevant identification for security checkpoints and anticipate random vehicle searches.</li> <li>Where possible, travel by air and minimise travel by road. Rural travel requires increased journey management planning, clear response measures and robust communications. Do not travel after dark.</li> <li>The above advice is not exhaustive; monitor our travel security alerts on Indonesia and contact our Assistance Centre for profile- and itinerary-specific advice prior to travel.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p>Around 1,800 police officers and additional military personnel remain deployed to and around the mountainous areas of Poso regency, a terrorist stronghold and alleged training base for the MIT. The aim of the operation is to capture the remaining MIT militants. Such operations are usually confined to rural areas, limiting their impact on business travellers, though additional checkpoints and patrols are also likely in urban centres to mitigate possible retaliatory attacks.\n<p>Over the past year, the security forces have arrested or killed at least 33 militants, including MIT leader Santoso, who was killed in July 2016. Most recently, on 31 December 2016, the National Police's counter-terrorism unit, Densus 88, arrested one suspected militant. The suspect was allegedly planning to carry out attacks during New Year's eve celebrations in different areas of the city of Palu (Central Sulawesi), including at churches and Shia Muslim religious places.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p>While the security operation has achieved notable success, there remains a persistent threat from extremist groups in Indonesia, some of which have ties with transnational organisations, such as the extremist Islamic State (IS) group, and share their ideology. However, it is unlikely that these links have significantly altered the capability of domestic militants to carry out attacks. Thus far, IS influence is currently limited to personal rather than organisational links with local affiliates, most likely facilitated by Indonesians who have interacted with foreign militants in the Middle East.\n<p>The primary targets of domestic militants continue to be the military, police and religious minorities. However, there are indications that local militants may attempt to replicate a pattern set by international jihadist groups, in which attacks are mounted against low-security areas, marketplaces, commercial districts, bars, and restaurants as well as transport hubs to maximise casualties. While any such incident is likely to be poorly planned because of the limited access to training, funds, and logistics support, even poorly trained militants with crude weapons can execute high-impact attacks, posing significant direct and indirect risks to travellers.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

There have been occasions during previous hearings, including on 13 February, when witnesses failed to appear to give their statements, leading to adjournments. </p>\n<p>The security force presence in the vicinity of the trial venue is likely to remain high due to precedents of the hardline Islamist group Islamic Defenders Front (FPI) holding large-scale rallies against Ahok for alleged blasphemy. The court is likely to restrict the number of spectators present at the hearings and conduct security checks on those entering the agriculture ministry building. Roads outside the complex will therefore be the main focus of any protests. Short-notice or partial closures of Jalan RM Harsono and nearby Jalan TB Simatupang are possible on hearing dates. While most demonstrations have passed off without incident, low-level scuffles between rival groups were reported during hearings on 3 January and 10 January. </p>\n<p><em>Security concerns</em> </p>\n<p>The North Jakarta district court was the venue for court hearings in December 2016, but they were moved to the agriculture ministry in January, primarily because it has a lower profile and is located further away from the <a href=\"https://goo.gl/maps/Vb6LC5X1ya62\" target=\"_blank\">Istana Merdeka</a> (presidential palace). This means that the presidential palace and nearby sensitive government institutions are unlikely to be affected by spillover violence if protests become unruly. </p>\n<p> Many of the shops near the North Jakarta district court also have local ethnic-Chinese owners. Minor episodes of vandalism and the looting of several local ethnic-Chinese-owned shops were reported on 4 November 2016 during a large anti-Ahok rally outside the presidential palace. Although the police said that the incidents were unrelated, the development reflects the underlying potential for racial and religious tensions to prompt unrest. However, incidents targeting Chinese expatriates or travellers, including low-level verbal harassment or physical assaults, remain highly unlikely.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Ahok, an ethnic-Chinese Christian, stands accused of insulting the Koran, the Islamic holy book, during a speech to prospective voters in late September 2016. Although he has apologised for causing offence, official investigations into the allegations against him have continued. Opposition to Ahok, who is campaigning for a second term in the current gubernatorial election, is most likely driven by politics rather than religious tensions.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/tsalertmaps/2017/february/indonesia-jakarta-28-february-trial-protest-20170224.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

The accomplice is still at large. </p>\n<p>The authorities can be expected to increase security around potential militant targets and carry out further security operations in the coming days. Domestic militant groups primarily target the military, the police and religious minorities. Any militant attacks are likely to be carried out by small, decentralised networks and typically involve the use of crude bombs or rudimentary weapons such as knives and small firearms. Although such attacks are likely to be poorly executed because of perpetrators' limited access to training, funds and logistical support, they can have major impact, posing significant direct and indirect risks to travellers.</p>\n<p>Islamist militants in Indonesia have shown growing intent to carry out high-impact and high-profile strikes against soft targets such as shopping centres, entertainment venues, open-air tourist sites, commercial and entertainment districts and public transport hubs. It is difficult for the authorities to detect and prevent one-off and small-scale attacks against lower-security soft targets where foreigners congregate, such as bars and restaurants; as such, members should exercise situational awareness at all times in these venues.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Liberia; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Lassa Fever<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Cases of Lassa fever, including at least two deaths, have been reported in the counties of Bong, Lofa and Nimba. Lassa fever is a viral haemorrhagic illness which is consistently present in Liberia. Transmission to humans mostly occurs through ingestion or inhalation of excretions from infected rodents, especially rats. Transmission can also occur through direct contact with the body fluids of an infected person. The disease is treated with antiviral medication. The risk to business travellers is low.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p><strong>Advice</strong></p>\n<ul> <li>Follow strict hygiene procedures, including frequent handwashing.</li> <li>Select safe food and water.</li> <li>Avoid contact with rodents and sick people.</li> <li>Seek medical care if you have been in an affected area and develop symptoms.</li> <li>Those at highest risk, working closely with infected patients, should discuss ribavirin prophylaxis with a specialist physician.</li>\n</ul>\n<p><br/>\n</p>\n<ul>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Since the beginning of January, over 10 confirmed and suspected cases of Lassa fever have been reported from the counties of Bong, Nimba and Lofa. There have been at least two fatalities in Nimba. Over 100 contacts have been identified and will undergo a 21 day observation period.</p>\n<p>Outbreaks of Lassa fever are common in Liberia and occur annually with highest incidence in Lofa, Nimba and Grand Cape Mount. Eighty percent of Liberia is estimated to be at risk for Lassa fever. In some areas of Liberia, approximately 15% of people admitted to hospitals every year have Lassa fever.</p>\n<p>\nWhat is Lassa Fever? </p>\n<p>Lassa virus is most common in West African countries. Rodents, particularly rats, carry the disease. People usually become infected by inhaling dried rodent excretions, eating food contaminated by rodent excrement, or eating infected rodents. Outbreaks occur when people unprotected contact with bodily fluids from an infected person, which can occur in households and in healthcare facilities. Symptoms commonly include fever, joint and back pain, sore throat, abdominal pain and diarrhoea. The disease causes severe illness in about 20 percent of those who contract it and it can be fatal, especially if treatment is delayed or unavailable. Severe Lassa fever is characterized by bleeding from the nose, mouth, gastrointestinal tract and vagina. The antiviral drug ribavirin may save a patient's life if given soon after infection. This drug is sometimes used as a preventative measure for people who have had extremely high-risk exposure to the Lassa virus. Prevention is through avoiding contact with rodents and their excretions, ensuring food and water are hygienically prepared, and using strict infection control practices when caring for sick people. </p>\n<p>For More Information: </p>\n<ul> <li>See \"Lassa Fever\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown.</li> <li>See Travel Advice Articles \"Selecting Safe Food and Water\" on the International SOS Country Guide.</li> <li>See the Travel Advice article \"Rodents spread diseases\" on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>About Liberia: see the International SOS Country Guide. </li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/february-2017/liberia_lassa_07feb2017.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

              "primary": "Medical Alert ",
              "sub": " Disease Outbreak"

          "long": 46.834717,
          "lat": -23.750154,
          "id": 951422,
          "version": 4.1,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Madagascar; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Plague<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Cases of plague continue to be reported in Atsimo-Atsinanana and Ihorombe regions of Madagascar. Many have died. Although plague is known to occur in Madagascar, the outbreak affected area lies outside of regions that commonly report cases. The disease is caused by a bacterium which mainly affects rodents. It is occasionally transmitted to humans when they are bitten by fleas carrying the bacteria, or more rarely via contact with an infected animal or person. Prevent infection by avoiding flea bites and maintaining strict hygiene precautions.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following if in an affected area:</p>\n<ul> <li>Observe strict hygiene measures.</li> <li>Avoid sick or dead animals.</li> <li>Avoid rodent nests or burrows.</li> <li>Take steps to avoid flea bites.</li> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks).</li> <li>Use an effective insect repellent, such as one containing DEET, Picaridin, PMD or IR 3535.</li>\n</ul></p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>An outbreak of plague is ongoing in Befotaka and Iakora districts in Atsimo-Atsinanana and Ihorombe regions, respectively. More than 60 cases have been reported in the outbreak suspected to have begun in mid-August last year, including at least 26 deaths. Cases of both pneumonic and bubonic plague have been confirmed. Investigation in neighbouring villages is underway. Health authorities, with assistance from the World Health Organization, have stepped up measures to contain the spread of disease.</p>\n<p>The disease is considered endemic in parts of Madagascar. Cases are frequently reported, particularly during the rainy season.</p>\n<p>What is Plague?</p>\n<p>Plague is found in many areas of the world. It is caused by the bacterium <em>Yersinia pestis</em> and mainly affects rodents. It is occasionally transmitted to humans when they are bitten by fleas carrying the bacteria, or more rarely via contact with an infected animal or person. There are three main forms of plague in humans: bubonic, septicaemic and pneumonic.</p>\n<p>Bubonic plague is the more common form and occurs as the result of a flea bite. The plague bacteria travel through the lymphatic system to the nearest lymph node. That lymph node becomes enlarged and tender, forming a \"bubo\".</p>\n<p>The septicaemic form of plague occurs when the infection spreads in the bloodstream. Without antibiotic treatment, this form of plague is usually fatal.</p>\n<p>Pneumonic plague occurs when <em>Y. pestis</em> infects the lungs. The first signs of pneumonic plague are fever, headache, weakness and cough that produces bloody or watery sputum. The pneumonia progresses over two to four days and may cause septic shock and, without early treatment, death. This form of plague can move from person to person, if someone has close contact with a plague patient. The sick person coughs out infectious respiratory droplets which can spread the bacteria to others. People who have had face-to-face contact with a pneumonic plague patient are given prophylactic (preventative) antibiotic treatment.\n</p>\n<p>For more information:\n</p>\n<ul> <li>About plague: See \"Plague\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides. </li> <li>About insect repellent: see the Travel Advice Article \"Using Insect Repellents Safely\" on the International SOS Country Guides. </li> <li>About the Madagascar: see the International SOS Country Guide. </li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance center.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>The outbreak is occurring in remote areas and risk of infection to travellers is low. The disease may spread to neighbouring regions.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of:</p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including plague, who is most at risk and prevention strategies.</li> <li>Who to contact for advice and treatment if they get sick during or after travel.</li> <li>How to access bite prevention resources including insect repellents.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Consider:</p>\n<ul> <li>Providing a travel health kit, including insect repellent.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/jan17/madagascar-plague.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

          "created": "2016-09-04T01:00:04Z",
          "modified": "2017-02-13T07:03:13Z",
          "categories": [
            {
              "primary": "Medical Alert ",
              "sub": " Disease Outbreak"
            }
          ],
          "long": 117,
          "lat": 5.5,
          "id": 948861,
          "version": 15,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Malaysia; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Zika virus<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Local transmission of Zika virus has been reported in parts of Malaysia including, Likas, Sabah and Johor Bahru. Increased surveillance and mosquito control activities are ongoing. Zika may be established in the local mosquito population however, the extent and intensity of spread is uncertain at this time. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. Sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms of Zika are usually mild and self-limited, there is a risk of severe and irreversible birth defects in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing. Those who are in affected areas should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission. After travel, ongoing precautions against sexual transmission are recommended. </p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing. In addition to the infectious risks of any destination, it is important to consider the standard of available healthcare, and the availability of obstetric and neonatal specialist support (should it be required).Pregnant women - discuss your risks with your doctor, including whether you should postpone travel.</p>\n<p>For those who will travel to, or are in Malaysia:</p>\n<ul> <li>Prevent infection by preventing mosquito bites: Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535. When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks). Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Consider using an insecticide-treated bed net. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room. Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible. </li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission through the use of condoms (or abstinence). </li>\n</ul>\n<p>\nAfter travel: </p>\n<ul> <li>Pregnant women - Consult your doctor. You may be advised to be tested for Zika whether or not you develop symptoms. Your doctor may also arrange additional monitoring or specialist care. </li> <li>All travellers - Monitor your health for two weeks. If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor and advise them of your travel history. This is especially important if you are, or are trying to fall pregnant. </li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission - Anyone who has a pregnant partner should use condoms (applicable to males and females) or abstain for the duration of the pregnancy. For those whose partners are not pregnant, authorities differ in their recommendations for the duration for which precautions should continue. <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">The World Health Organization advises</a> that<strong> everyone</strong> should continue precautions for at least 6 months after return. <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/prevention/protect-yourself-during-sex.html\" target=\"_blank\">The United States CDC advises</a>: <strong>Men</strong> should continue precautions for at least 6 months; <strong>Women</strong> should continue precautions for at least 8 weeks. </li> <li>Consider delaying pregnancy - The World Health Organization <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> couples or women planning a pregnancy, who have returned from an area with Zika transmission, to wait at least 6 months. The United States CDC <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/pregnancy/women-and-their-partners.html\" target=\"_blank\">recommend</a> women trying to get pregnant wait at least 8 weeks and partners of women trying to get pregnant wait at least 6 months.</li> <li>Prevent transmission to local mosquitoes. If you are returning to an area that has mosquitoes which can transmit Zika, continue to prevent mosquito bites for 2 to 3 weeks (e.g. using insect repellent). This will reduce the risk of infecting local mosquitoes with Zika, and therefore reduce the risk of an outbreak.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Since the beginning of September there have been at least eight confirmed cases of Zika, including an imported case from Singapore. Testing on five of the Zika patients indicate that the virus contracted locally is from the \"Asian lineage\". Two cases, including the imported case, are similar to the post-2007 strain, currently circulating in French Polynesia since 2013. The other three cases are from the older “Asian” lineage which has been previously circulating in Southeast Asia.</p>\n<p>Zika may have been present in mosquitoes in Malaysia for some time. A report <a href=\"http://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/21/5/14-1960_article\" target=\"_blank\">published in May 2015</a> confirms Zika infection in a traveller who visited Sabah and peninsular Malaysia in 2014.</p>\n<p>What is Zika?</p>\n<p>Zika is a viral disease, mostly transmitted to people by mosquito bites. Zika virus can be passed from an infected pregnant mother to her baby. Spread through sexual contact occurs. Transmission via blood transfusion has been reported. Most people who have Zika infections do not have any symptoms. If symptoms occur, they are similar to chikungunya or dengue, and include fever, rash, joint and muscle pains or conjunctivitis (red eyes), and typically last up to a week. The virus can cause severe irreversible birth defects, such as \"microcephaly\" (a small head with developmental abnormalities), and serious neurological complications. There is no specific treatment available, and no vaccine to prevent the disease.</p>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See the <a href=\"http://pandemic.internationalsos.com/zika\" target=\"_blank\">International SOS Zika website</a>. </li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on Malaysia.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>The situation is continuing to evolve. The geographic spread within Malaysia, and transmission intensity is uncertain. An increase in cases is likely as screening and testing is increased. Further cases may be sporadic in nature, however an outbreak with high case numbers and rapid growth might occur.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

The authorities have warned that this year's late start to the monsoon season means that Kelantan, Pahang, Sabah, Sarawak and Terengganu states could experience intermittent flooding into March.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

The abductors fled towards the border with Burkina Faso; two suspects were later arrested by the authorities. No group has yet claimed responsibility for the kidnapping, which underlines the need to adopt stringent precautions in border areas.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Members undertaking travel to border areas of Sikasso region should adopt enhanced security precautions. This should include liaising with local officials and embassies, as well as robust journey management planning and obtaining itinerary-specific advice prior to any travel. </li> <li>Avoid all travel to the northern desert regions of Gao, Kidal and Timbuktu, as well as to areas of Mopti region located north of Mopti city and Segou region north of Segou city, due to the risks associated with Islamist militancy. </li> <li>Vary routines as a security precaution. Try to change itineraries and timings for regular journeys. </li> <li>Liaise with local contacts and monitor our travel security alerts for Mali for further developments.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The kidnap illustrates the deterioration of the security environment in eastern Mali and follows the abductions of a French NGO worker in Gao region (December 2016) and a Swiss national in Timbuktu (December 2015). Jihadist activity in those areas informs their EXTREME travel risk ratings. Sikasso region carries a MEDIUM travel risk rating, as militant operations there have hitherto been infrequent.</p>\n<p>Nonetheless, northern Sikasso, where Karangasso is located, borders both Mopti region – where Islamist militant activity has increased over the past year, and the travel risks are HIGH – and Burkina Faso. Proximity to a porous international frontier can also facilitate kidnappers' access and escape.</p>\n<p>The increased militant presence in Segou and Mopti regions means that the reach of such groups has potentially expanded further south, posing increased risks of hit-and-run attacks in north-eastern parts of Sikasso. The development has already translated into a series of security incidents in northern Segou and in Mopti, including numerous attacks on security force personnel.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Sikasso experienced some militant activity in mid-2015 – including an attack on Misséni in June – but no further incidents occurred after military operations in the area, near the border with Côte d'Ivoire, were carried out. However, Malian forces lack the capabilities to effectively counter militancy, especially in remote, sparsely populated and poorly policed eastern and northern areas.</p> <p>Mali, Burkina Faso, Chad, Mauritania and Niger earlier in February said that they would create a joint counter-terrorism force, though no further details were provided. The UN Mission for the Stabilization of Mali (MINUSMA) comprises 12,000 troops, while 3,000 French soldiers are also deployed across the region. Nevertheless, efforts to eradicate jihadist activity in northern desert regions have been hampered by lawlessness, conflict, instability in Libya and continued smuggling of weapons across the Sahel.</p> </p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

The figure marks around a 34% increase over January 2016 and is the highest number of homicides in any January since 1997. At least 25 of 32 states experienced some sort of increase, though the greatest comparative percentage rises occurred in Aguascalientes, Baja California Sur, Colima, Durango, Hidalgo, San Luis Potosi, Tabasco and Yucatan. The increase in homicides underscores the persistent risks posed by violent crime in Mexico and the need for travellers to exercise precautions at all times.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to HIGH travel risk areas should be for essential purposes only and should be arranged in close liaison with trusted local contacts. Do not attempt to cross any roadblocks without permission, as this may elicit a hostile response.</li> <li>Criminals are often armed; take stringent security precautions at all times. If accosted by a criminal, assume that the assailant is armed and do nothing to resist or antagonise them.</li> <li>Do not undertake travel on foot after dark in HIGH risk areas to mitigate risk of exposure to opportunistic crime.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on Mexico for further information. </li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The latest figures continue a gradual trend from the previous year affecting a majority of states. While there were also notable rises elsewhere, the states of Colima, Baja California Sur, Guerrero, Sinaloa, Baja California and Chihuahua had the highest per-capita incidence of murder for the January period. </p>\n<p>Although travellers are rarely targeted in drug-related violence, increased homicide rates can result in an overall destabilisation of the travel security environment in affected locations. The development, and the planned extradition of the captive to the US, are unlikely to significantly improve the security environment in Chihuahua, where the Sinaloa cartel retains a significant operational presence. We continue to rate the risks associated with travel to the state as HIGH and all travel there should be for essential purposes only.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to HIGH travel risk areas should be for essential purposes only and should be arranged in close liaison with trusted local contacts. </li> <li>Members should carefully plan all aspects of their itinerary and be confident in their accommodation, transport, communication and security arrangements prior to travelling. </li> <li>Recommended measures include travelling with a private vehicle and security-trained driver and undertaking movement in daylight hours only. Keep windows up and doors locked at all times, and do not undertake road travel outside urban centres after dark. </li> <li>Given the difficulties associated with arranging secure accommodation in US border areas, avoid overnight stays and arrange lodging in the US where possible. </li> <li>Seek expert itinerary- and profile-specific advice prior to travel to Mexico.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Hernandez was captured in the latest security operation targeting leaders of the Sinaloa cartel, the most high-profile of which, in January 2016, resulted in the detention and extradition to the US of the group's former leader Joaquin Guzman (alias ‘El Chapo'). However, precedent suggests that the arrest of high-profile cartel leaders by itself is unlikely to substantially affect the overall travel security environment in Chihuahua.</p> <p>The state has long been affected by high levels of drug-related violence due to its strategically valuable location for traffickers seeking to move narcotics across the US border. A 75% increase in homicides, attributed by the authorities to organised crime groups active in the state, was reported in Chihuahua during January and February. Although cartel-related activity is unlikely to target travellers on legitimate business, shoot-outs between rival gangs, or between gang members and the security forces, can occur in public areas and pose a substantial incidental risk to bystanders.</p>\n</p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Afonso Dhlakama, the leader of the armed opposition movement Mozambican National Resistance (Renamo), announced the extension on 3 March to allow further peace talks with President Filipe Nyusi's government. The original ceasefire has been extended multiple times to accommodate dialogue with the government. Despite this positive development, some insurgent-led violence is likely to continue.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to Mozambique can continue with standard security precautions.</li> <li>Avoid all non-essential travel to rural areas of Sofala, Manica, Zambezia provinces because of the increased risks posed there by insurgent activity.</li> <li>Road movement in these areas should be for business-critical purposes only and should be conducted with experienced local drivers, a strict communications regime and security support. Members can continue to fly to or between urban centres, including provincial capitals, without additional precautions.</li> <li>Members intending to conduct business-critical road journeys should contact their nearest Assistance Centre for an up-to-date assessment and itinerary-specific advice. Expect delays along routes due to enforced convoy procedures on certain highways.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Amid an increase in violence in 2016, talks between Renamo and the government resumed in July; this was the first time the two parties had negotiated since August 2015. Incidents have been reported in at least six central and northern provinces, where Renamo frequently targeted commercial and private vehicles. After a spate of attacks on vehicles, the authorities introduced mandatory security force convoys along EN1 highway in Sofala in February 2016. Similar requirements were introduced along the EN7 highway in Manica in June.</p>\n<p>However, the convoys were mostly ineffective in deterring the attacks. A civilian bus under military escort on 12 December was attacked along the EN7 in Barue district (Manica province), where days prior at least two other attacks were reported. The same week, vehicles transporting a group of foreign nationals were attacked on the road between the EN1 and Inhamitanga (Sofala province). Renamo also started targeting state-owned property in remote villages, such as health centres, police stations and government offices. Violence linked to the Renamo insurgency continues to pose direct and indirect risks to travellers.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Dengue outbreaks are also ongoing in other Pacific islands. The disease is consistently present in Nauru. Dengueis spread by mosquitoes and can cause a range of symptoms. It has no particular treatment. Some people, especially those who have been infected before, get a more severe form that can lead to fatal complications. Although a vaccine has been developed, it is only available in a small number of countries and is not recommended for travellers. The protests are to denounce a government decision to hold local elections on 14 May.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to the Terai region, which has a HIGH travel risk rating, can continue with heightened awareness and flexible itineraries.</li> <li>Liaise with local contacts for information on protests. Avoid all demonstrations due to the risk of disturbances. In the event of unrest, where possible to do so safely, return to your accommodation and remain there until the situation has stabilised.</li> <li>Overland travel in Terai should be conducted only after confirming that planned routes are clear and with a robust journey management plan in place.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on Nepal for related updates.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>A shutdown strike was held on 22 February which disrupted central and eastern Terai district. The East-West Highway was obstructed by protesters.</p>\n<p>Low level unrest, including minor scuffles, the burning of tyres and stone-throwing from protesters were reported in various cities such as Birgunj and Rajbiraj. Clashes between supporters of Samyukta Loktantrik Madhesi Morcha (SLMM) and the Unified Communist Party of Nepal-Maoist (UCPN-M) occurred in Janakpur (Janakpur zone) early on 22 February. Meanwhile, hundreds of people were stranded in their vehicles in Morang while markets in Kalaiya, Simraungadh, Parwanipur, Kolhabi and Jitpur were closed throughout the day.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>The duration and intensity of protests varies between districts and is often accompanied by shutdown strikes (bandhs); such activity can result in significant disruption to road travel and commercial activity, particularly in Terai. Rallies by Madhesi activists often involve sit-ins and blockades of regional highways, often rendering inter-city road travel impractical for several hours. In addition, demonstrations usually degenerate into clashes between rival activists and/or protesters and the police.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Clashes erupted when an ethnic-Madhesi group attempted to disrupt a rally by the main opposition Communist Party of Nepal Unified Marxist-Leninist (CPN-UML). Police shot dead three members of the United Democratic Madhesi Front (UDMF) in the ensuing disturbances, prompting an ethnic-Madhesi bandh (shutdown strike) <strong>until at least 8 March</strong> across the district. Madhesi groups have also been involved in scuffles with the police in other parts of Saptari as well as Parsa district. </p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Avoid all protests due to the potential for clashes between protesters and the police. If protesters or security force personnel begin to gather, return to your accommodation or the nearest secure location and remain there until the situation stabilises.</li> <li>Liaise with local contacts for information on the timing and location of rallies. Avoid all demonstrations as basic security precaution. If you encounter unrest, where possible to do so safely, return to your accommodation and remain there until the situation stabilises.</li> <li>Reconfirm the status of planned travel routes before setting out due to the potential for roadblocks. Do not attempt to cross any roadblocks, as this may provoke a hostile reaction from protesters. Instead, turn around and seek an alternative route to your destination or wait at a safe distance for protesters to disperse.</li> <li>Travel to the Terai region, which has a HIGH travel risk rating, can continue with flexible itineraries and heightened awareness due to the latent risk of widespread and disruptive protests. Overland travel should be conducted after confirming that planned routes are clear and with a robust journey management plan in place.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on Nepal for related updates.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>UDMF members attempting to disrupt the 6 March CPN-UML rally were dispersed by the security forces using tear gas and live fire; as well as the three killed, at least a dozen people were injured. This included several police officers hit by stones. The CPN-UML, which has long had tense relations with ethnic-Madhesi groups, had launched a 15-day political campaign on 4 March to oppose a controversial constitutional amendment bill (<em>see our separate report</em>). This campaign will now be postponed until 10 March; further unrest is liable at future CPN-UML gatherings. </p>\n<p>The main ethnic-Madhesi group, the SLMM, joined the UDMF in the shutdown strike that began in Saptari on 7 March to denounce the previous day's police shooting. Related protests also occurred in Birgunj (Parsa), where the authorities subsequently heightened security, and Lahan (Siraha district). Demonstrators set fire to a vehicle at a Nepalgunj (Banke district) market for allegedly breaking the bandh and have attempted to disrupt the Gulariya-Nepalganj Highway. The police may use tough crowd-control measures to disperse any further unruly crowds and dismantle any prolonged highway blockades. Any such action would increase the chance of bystanders facing incidental risks.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>New Caledonia (France); </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Dengue fever<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>The outbreak of dengue fever continues to intensify across New Caledonia. Dengue outbreaks are also ongoing in other Pacific islands. The disease is spread by mosquitoes, and these inhabit both rural and urban areas. Dengue can cause a range of symptoms and has no particular treatment. Some people, especially those who have been infected before, get a more severe form that can lead to fatal complications. Although a vaccine has been developed, it is only available in a small number of countries and is not recommended for travellers. Prevent infection by avoiding mosquito bites.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<ul> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks).</li> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535.</li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room.</li> <li>Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.</li> <li>Seek medical attention if you develop a high fever, especially if you suffer \"rigors\" (shaking) or a rash.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The dengue outbreak is increasing nationwide, with over one thousand cases since the outbreak began and more than 100 new cases each week. The capital city of Noumea is heavily affected, with Dumbea and Mont-Dore also reporting more than 100 cases each. Dengue outbreaks are occurring on other islands in the South Pacific and travellers between the islands can introduce the virus to local mosquitoes.</p> <p>For more information:</p> <ul> <li>About dengue: see \"Dengue Fever\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>About preventing mosquito bites: see the Travel Advice articles \"Preventing Mosquito Bites\" and \"Using Insect Repellents Safely\" on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on New Caledonia.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li> </ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

All travellers from countries designated by the World Health Organization (WHO) as having a risk of yellow fever transmission, with some additions and exceptions, are now required to show proof of yellow fever vaccination to enter Nicaragua. The vaccine must have been administered at least ten days prior to arrival. Previously, officials in Nicaragua did not require travellers to be vaccinated against yellow fever before entry into the country.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travellers arriving from these countries should be vaccinated at least ten days prior to arrival.</li> <li>Carry the International Certificate of Vaccination or Prophylaxis in your hand luggage, and show it to authorities if requested.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>For More Information: </p>\n<ul> <li>See \"yellow fever\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guide. </li> <li>About Nicaragua: see the International SOS Country Guide. </li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Health authorities have decreed that all travellers from <a href=\"https://www.internationalsos.com/MasterPortal/Article.aspx?languageid=ENG&amp;ContentID=7\" target=\"_blank\">countries considered to have a risk of yellow fever</a>, <strong>including</strong> Argentina, Bolivia, Brazil, Colombia, Ecuador, French Guiana, Panama, Venezuela, Paraguay and Peru, AND all African countries <strong>except</strong> the Democratic Republic of Congo, Tanzania, Sao Tome and Principe, and Somalia are required to show proof of yellow fever vaccination to enter Nicaragua. </p>\n<p>This is to prevent travellers bringing yellow fever into Nicaragua, which currently does not have a risk of yellow fever transmission. Travellers who fail to comply with requirements, or cannot provide a valid waiver, may be prevented from entering the country.<br/>\n<br/>\nYellow Fever Vaccination\n<br/>\nVaccination is only available through designated yellow fever vaccination clinics. These clinics issue a signed and stamped International Certificate of Vaccination or Prophylaxis (ICVP) which becomes valid 10 days after the vaccination, and is valid for the lifetime of the person vaccinated. </p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

The attack is the latest in a series of incidents that prompted the government on 3 March to declare a state of emergency in parts of Tahoua and Tillaberi regions. Members are reminded of the need to avoid all journeys to the HIGH travel risk areas within 100 miles (160km) of the Malian border.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Avoid all journeys to HIGH travel risk areas within 100 miles (160km) of the border with Mali, because of the risks posed by militant activity. Travel to the northern region should be for essential purposes only and requires stringent security measures. </li> <li>Members travelling to rural areas north of the capital Niamey and within 125 miles (200km) of the Malian border should adopt stringent security precautions. </li> <li>There is a credible risk of terrorist attacks by Islamist extremists in Niger. Where possible, minimise time spent in the vicinity of potential targets for militant attacks, including embassies, government and security force assets and entertainment venues where Westerners are known to congregate. </li> <li>If undertaking business-critical travel outside major urban areas, register with your embassy and all relevant warden networks and ensure you have comprehensive security and journey management planning in place beforehand, as well as a secure escort. Government escorts are highly recommended for overland travel outside Niamey. </li> <li>The above advice is not exhaustive; seek itinerary-specific advice prior to travel to Niger.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Reports indicate that the attack was eventually repelled by the military, prompting the assailants to retreat towards the Malian border. Additional security force personnel and aircraft were deployed during follow-up security operations. Although no group has claimed responsibility for the attack, Islamist militants based in Mali are the most likely perpetrators. The incident follows a recent wave of raids on security posts by gunmen in desert areas in or bordering Mali. In particular, a Mali-based group affiliated to the extremist Islamic State (IS) movement has been blamed for previous incidents.</p>\n<p>The 3 March state of emergency applies in Abala, Ayorou, Banibangou, Bankilare and Ouallam departments (all Tillaberi), as well as Tassara and Tilia (both Tahoua region). The measure grants the security forces additional powers, including the ability to conduct house searches at any time. However, despite international efforts to support counter-terrorism operations, Niger's security forces are overstretched and lack the capability to effectively counter militancy and banditry in remote areas.</p> </p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>The latest incident underlines the continued potential for militant incursions and cross-border attacks in areas near the Malian border, justifying the region's HIGH travel risk rating. The recent emergence of IS in the Sahel is a particular cause for concern amid an increase in Islamist extremist activity linked to several factors, including setbacks experienced by IS' Libyan branch in Sirte (Libya). Niger borders Libya to the north and is exposed to infiltration by militants using the Sahel's traditional trafficking corridors in southern Libya and northern Niger.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Nigeria; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Lassa Fever<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>The Lassa fever outbreak continues to expand, with cases reported in at least twelve states. Dozens of fatalities have been recorded. Authorities are responding to prevent further spread. Lassa fever is a viral haemorrhagic fever, transmitted to humans on contact with excretions from infected rodents. Outbreaks occur through direct contact with body fluids of an infected person. Risk to the business traveller is low. Prevent infection through observing strict hygiene and avoiding contact with sick people.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p> <ul> <li>Follow strict hygiene procedures, including frequent handwashing.</li> <li>Avoid risky exposures : Do not participate in traditional funerals. Avoid direct unprotected contact with sick people and their body fluids. Avoid environments contaminated by rodents. Select safe food and water.</li> <li>If you do need to seek medical care for any reason, call International SOS and we will direct you to an appropriate facility.</li> </ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Since December 2016, there have been over 60 confirmed cases of Lassa fever, including more than 35 deaths. Twelve states have reported cases: Bauchi, Borno, Cross River, Ebonyi, Edo, Kaduna, Nasarawa, Ogun, Ondo, Plateau, Rivers and Taraba. The Nigerian Centre of Disease Control (NCDC) is assisting the affected states in their response activities. The Ministry of Health has implemented a rapid response, raising awareness in healthcare workers to use appropriate infection control procedures, test before treating for malaria, and have a high index of suspicion especially if malaria tests are negative. </p>\n<p>Lassa fever is persistently present in Nigeria, and outbreaks are not uncommon.</p>\n<p>What is Lassa Fever?\n</p>\n<p>Lassa virus is most common in West African countries. Rodents, particularly rats, carry the disease. People usually become infected by inhaling dried rodent excretions, eating food contaminated by rodent excrement, or eating infected rodents. Outbreaks occur when people unprotected contact with bodily fluids from an infected person, which can occur in households and in healthcare facilities. Symptoms commonly include fever, joint and back pain, sore throat, abdominal pain and diarrhoea. The disease causes severe illness in about 20 percent of those who contract it and it can be fatal, especially if treatment is delayed or unavailable. Severe Lassa fever is characterized by bleeding from the nose, mouth, gastrointestinal tract and vagina. The antiviral drug ribavirin may save a patient's life if given soon after infection. This drug is sometimes used as a preventative measure for people who have had extremely high-risk exposure to the Lassa virus.\nPrevention is through avoiding contact with rodents and their excretions, ensuring food and water are hygienically prepared, and using strict infection control practices when caring for sick people.</p>\n<p>For More Information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See \"Lassa Fever\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>About Nigeria: see the International SOS Country Guide.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Further cases may be identified. As the rapid response procedure has been implemented, it is expected the outbreak will be contained.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>Ensure your travellers (and other employees if relevant) are aware of:</p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including Lassa fever and malaria.</li> <li>What to do if they get sick during and after travel.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/march-2017/nigeria-lassa-3-mar-2017.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

In Islamabad, security measures have been increased in Sectors F-6, F-7, F-10, G-6 as well as the Blue Area, the main commercial hub, and the Red Zone, where government buildings, the diplomatic enclave, as well as upscale business-class hotels used by international travellers and the Pakistani elite are located. </p>\n<p>However, International SOS and Control Risks have confirmed with their team in Islamabad that, contrary to some rumours on social media, no curfew has been imposed in the capital and the police have not issued advice to avoid any specific sites in the city. </p>\n<p>Stringent security measures and increased security checks can be expected around potential militant targets in major cities and key routes around the country. The Sindh provincial authorities on 23 February also issued an alert, warning that key public places in Karachi might be targets, including the Sindh High Court, Sindh Assembly, Empress Market, Zainab Market, markets at MA Jinnah Road and restaurants at Do Darya in DHA along Sea View Road. Frequent counter-terrorism operations can also be expected by the security forces; around 30 militants have been killed in such raids across the country in recent days. However, the authorities subsequently confirmed that the alerts were hoaxes. </p>\n<p>Nonetheless, there is a credible risk of terrorist attacks in Pakistan. Militants continue to primarily focus on security force and government infrastructure and personnel as well as religious facilities associated with minorities. However, militants remain active in major urban centres and have shown a willingness to carry out attacks against soft targets such as marketplaces, parks, and other areas that have minimal security and a large number of individuals gathered. Attacks against high security facilities such as business class hotels, international schools and embassies, while impossible to rule out, remain significantly less likely. </p>\n<p>Several militant groups maintain recruiting and operational cells in the country, including the Islamist Tehreek-e-Taliban Pakistan (TTP, the Pakistani Taliban), Lashkar-e-Jhangvi (LeJ), as well as the international terrorist network al-Qaida. Given the country's entrenched terrorist network, the risk of further successful militant attacks will persist in Pakistan.</p>\n<p>Details of major attacks over the past week are as follows:</p>\n<ul> <li>16 February: A suicide bombing at the Lal Shahbaz Qalandar Sufi Muslim shrine in Sehwan Sharif killed at least 75 people and injured more than 200 others. </li> <li>15 February: A suicide attack outside the Peshawar Development Authority (PDA) office in the Hayatabad area of Peshawar killed at least two person and injuring four civil judges.</li> <li>13 February: Two police officers were killed while defusing a bomb near a bus stop on Sariab Road in Quetta (Balochistan province); nine people were injured.</li> <li>13 February: A suicide bombing near the Provincial Assembly of Punjab on Mall Road in Lahore. This is the first time cases of the disease have been confirmed in the country. Chikungunya can cause a severe illness, particularly in newborn babies, older adults, and people with underlying health issues (such as diabetes, heart disease, weakened immune system). Prevent infection by avoiding mosquito bites. Chikungunya can cause a severe illness, particularly in newborn babies, older adults, and people with underlying health issues (such as diabetes, heart disease, weakened immune system). Prevent infection by avoiding mosquito bites.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:\n</p>\n<ul> <li>Anyone who is at higher risk of severe illness should discuss their itinerary with their doctor for individual advice.</li> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks).</li> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535.</li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. The areas of the city most affected are Lyari, Malir, Saudabad and Shah Faisal. No fatalities were recorded. The Pakistan Office of the World Health Organisation has issued a <a href=\"http://www.emro.who.int/images/stories/WHO_Chikungunya_Health_Advisory.pdf?ua=1\" target=\"_blank\">Chikungunya Health Advisory</a></p>\n<p>Chikungunya infection has been previously documented in many parts of Asia.</p>\n<p>What is chikungunya?</p>\n<p>Chikungunya is a viral disease transmitted via mosquito bites. Where the disease is known to occur, infections are usually common during the rainy season. It cannot pass from person to person. The first symptom is usually a sudden onset of fever. Severe joint pain is one of the disease's main symptoms - chikungunya is Swahili for \"that which contorts or bends up\". Headache and rash are also common. Symptoms are similar to dengue, and laboratory testing is required to confirm the diagnosis.</p>\n<p>There is no cure for chikungunya and it is rarely fatal. Most people recover over several weeks. The associated joint pain can last for several months, or even years. The very young, older adults and people with underlying health conditions are at higher risk for a severe illness. There is no specific treatment. Aspirin and non-steroidal anti-inflammatory medications (such as ibuprofen and naproxen sodium) can increase the risk of complications and should be avoided. </p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Further cases are likely to be reported. There is a risk of spread of the disease to surrounding areas.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of:</p> <ul> <li>Regional health threats.</li> <li>Higher risk groups for severe chikungunya illness</li> <li>Mosquito bite prevention measures.</li> <li>What to do if they get sick during and after travel.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Consider:</p>\n<ul> <li>Providing a travel health kit.</li> <li>Offering a pre-travel health assessment for those who are at higher risk of severe disease.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Pakistan; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Terror attack, Terror warning, Terrorism<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>The US and Australian governments on 2-3 March issued alerts warning of potential militant attacks on international and local English-speaking schools in Pakistan, following recent high-profile terrorist incidents across the country. Heightened security should be expected in the coming days around potential targets, including government buildings, educational institutions and crowded public areas, in the capital Islamabad and other major cities such as Karachi (Sindh province), Lahore, Rawalpindi (both Punjab province) and Peshawar (Khyber Pakhtunkhwa province). Members should observe precautions and comply with all security force directives.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to most parts of Pakistan can continue with stringent security measures; these include maintaining a low profile and implementing a thorough and robust journey management plan. </li> <li>Travellers should avoid crowded areas, such as open air-markets and public transport hubs, and minimise time spent around likely targets for militant attacks, both stationary and in transit; these include: security force personnel and installations, mosques, Shia Muslim and Christian religious gatherings, and densely populated unsecured areas such as markets, shopping malls and hotels. </li> <li>Although hotels are a potential target for Islamist militant attacks, we continue to advise business travellers to stay at international-standard hotels in Pakistan due to robust security measures in place. Minimise exposure to more likely targets including the lobby, car park and entrance. </li> <li>Anticipate increased scrutiny, including additional police patrols and spot-checks. Comply promptly with all instructions issued by the security forces and carry personal identification documents to facilitate movement through checkpoints. </li> <li>Monitor our alerts on Pakistan for further developments.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Despite a series of terrorist incidents during February, there are no indications of an imminent militant plot. It is more likely that the alerts were issued amid concerns over militant attacks in retaliation for an ongoing counter-terrorism operation launched in response to recent incidents.</p>\n<p>Militant groups remain active in major urban centres in Pakistan; their operations have primarily focused on security force and government infrastructure and personnel, as well as religious minorities, though soft targets such as markets, parks and other low-security areas where large crowds gather are also vulnerable. Attacks targeting high-security facilities such as business-class hotels, international schools and diplomatic missions, while impossible to rule out, remain significantly less likely.</p>\n<p><em>Security measures</em></p>\n<p>Stringent security checks and other measures can be expected around potential targets in major cities and key routes around the country. In Karachi, the authorities have identified the Sindh High Court, Sindh Assembly, Empress Market, Zainab Market, markets at MA Jinnah Road and restaurants at Do Darya in DHA on Sea View Road as potential targets.</p>\n<p>In Peshawar and Khyber Pakhtunhwa provinces, joint checkpoints have been established on the M1 route connecting Peshawar to Rawalpindi and Islamabad, as well as on the M2 linking Lahore to Rawalpindi and Islamabad. In the capital, security has been increased in Sectors F-6, F-7, F-10 and G-6, as well as in the Blue Area, the main commercial hub, and the Red Zone, where government buildings, the diplomatic enclave and upscale business-class hotels are located.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Several militant groups maintain recruiting and operational cells in the country, including the Tehreek-e-Taliban Pakistan (TTP, the Pakistani Taliban), Lashkar-e-Jhangvi (LeJ) and the international terrorist network al-Qaida. Meanwhile, the extremist Islamic State (IS) group's claim of responsibility for a 16 February bombing in Sehwan Sharif (Sindh) underlines the Middle East-based terrorist group's increasing operational capabilities in Pakistan.</p> <p>While IS is not known to have a strong presence in the country, a number of defectors from domestic militant organisations are believed to have pledged allegiance to the group. Given Pakistan's entrenched terrorist network, the risk of further successful militant attacks will persist.</p>\n</p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"
