Cases are anticipated to rise further. Dengue is consistently present in the country. The disease is spread by mosquitoes, and is present in both rural and urban areas. Dengue can cause a range of symptoms and has no particular treatment. Some people, especially those who have been infected before, get a more severe form that can lead to fatal complications. Prevent infection by avoiding mosquito bites.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<ul> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks).</li> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535.</li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room.</li> <li>Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.</li> <li>Seek medical attention if you develop a high fever, especially if you suffer \"rigors\" (shaking) or a rash.</li>\n</ul>\nFor more information:<br/>\n<ul> <li>About dengue: see \"Dengue Fever\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>About preventing mosquito bites: see the Travel Advice articles \"Preventing Mosquito Bites\" and \"Using Insect Repellents Safely\" on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on Palau.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/jan17/palaudengue.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

Ensure that they can access the police in the event of an emergency.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>We continue to rate the overall risks associated with travel to Panama as LOW, with the main risk to travellers arising from petty and opportunistic crimes such as pickpocketing and bag-snatching. Violent crimes, such as armed robberies and mugging, are less common but periodically occur throughout the country.</p>\n<p>Panama's homicide rate of 9.3 per 100,000 people in 2016 was the lowest in the last 11 years, with three murders recorded in Bocas del Toro province during this period. Government statistics indicate that the majority of homicides are linked to organised crime. Foreigners are generally not directly affected by such violence, though shoot-outs between members of criminal gangs in public areas pose a low but credible incidental risk.</p> </p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "version": 18,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Philippines; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Zika virus<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Locally-acquired cases of Zika infection continue to be detected in parts of Philippines, including in the National Capital Region (Manila). Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. While less common, sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms are usually mild and self-limited, severe and irreversible birth defects may occur in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider delaying travel to Zika-affected areas. Those who are in affected areas should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission. After travel to the affected areas, ongoing precautions against sexual transmission are recommended.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel. Many health authorities advise pregnant women not to travel to areas with active transmission of Zika virus.</p>\n<p>For those who will travel to, or are in the Philippines:</p>\n<ul> <li>Prevent infection by preventing mosquito bites: Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535. When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks). Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Consider using an insecticide-treated bed net. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room. Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.</li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission through the use of condoms (or abstinence).</li>\n</ul>\n<p>After travel:</p>\n<ul> <li>Pregnant women - Consult your doctor. You may be advised to be tested for Zika whether or not you develop symptoms. Your doctor may also arrange additional monitoring or specialist care.</li> <li>All travellers - Monitor your health for two weeks. If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor and advise them of your travel history. This is especially important if you are, or are trying to fall pregnant.</li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission - Anyone who has a pregnant partner should use condoms (applicable to males and females) or abstain for the duration of the pregnancy. For those whose partners are not pregnant, authorities differ in their recommendations for the duration for which precautions should continue. <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">The World Health Organization advises</a> that everyone should continue precautions for at least 6 months after return. <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/prevention/protect-yourself-during-sex.html\" target=\"_blank\">The United States CDC advises</a>: <strong>Men</strong> should continue precautions for at least 6 months; <strong>Women</strong> to continue precautions for at least 8 weeks.</li> <li>Consider delaying pregnancy - The World Health Organization <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> couples or women planning a pregnancy, who have returned from an area with Zika transmission, to wait at least 6 months. The United States CDC <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/pregnancy/women-and-their-partners.html\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> women trying to get pregnant wait at least 8 weeks and partners of women trying to get pregnant wait at least 6 months.</li> <li>Prevent transmission to local mosquitoes. If you are returning to an area that has mosquitoes which can transmit Zika, continue to prevent mosquito bites for 2 to 3 weeks (e.g. using insect repellent). This will reduce the risk of infecting local mosquitoes with Zika, and therefore reduce the risk of an outbreak.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>At least 57 cases of locally acquired Zika infection have been confirmed since September 2016. Cases have been reported from the regions of Calabarzon, Central Luzon, Central Visayas, Western Visayas, and the NCR. Prior to this, locally-transmitted Zika cases were reported in 2012. Sporadic cases have been documented since, including in travellers visiting Philippines.</p> <p>Authorities anticipate further cases and have intensified mosquito control activities. Zika may be established in the local mosquito population in parts of the Philippines, however the extent and intensity of spread is uncertain at this time.</p> <p>What is Zika? <br/> Zika is a viral disease, mostly transmitted to people by mosquito bites. Zika virus can be passed from an infected pregnant mother to her baby. Spread through sexual contact occurs. Transmission via blood transfusion has been reported. Most people who have Zika infections do not have any symptoms. If symptoms occur, they are similar to chikungunya or dengue, and include fever, rash, joint and muscle pains or conjunctivitis (red eyes), and typically last up to a week. The virus can cause severe irreversible birth defects, such as \"microcephaly\" (a small head with developmental abnormalities), and serious neurological complications. There is no specific treatment available, and no vaccine to prevent the disease. <br/> <br/> For more information: </p> <ul> <li>See the <a href=\"https://pandemic.internationalsos.com/zika\" target=\"_blank\">International SOS Zika website</a> </li> <li>See th International SOS Country Guide for Philippines</li> </ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Further cases are likely to occur.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of </p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including Zika, who is most at risk (such as pregnant women), and prevention strategies. </li> <li>Who to contact for advice and treatment if they get sick during or after travel. </li> <li>How to access bite prevention resources including insect repellent and bed nets. </li>\n</ul>\n<p>Consider: </p>\n<ul> <li>Providing a travel health kit, including insect repellent. </li> <li>Stipulating a medical pre-travel consultation.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/february-2017/philizika.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

While the move is not indicative of increased travel security risks, low-level clashes between the security forces and rebels are expected to persist, particularly where the NPA is active, namely <a href=\"https://goo.gl/maps/Ut4xKHWHEML2\" target=\"_blank\">Agusan del Sur</a>, <a href=\"https://goo.gl/maps/DUvZFePC1yz\" target=\"_blank\">Compostela Valley</a>, <a href=\"https://goo.gl/maps/iZa9Eg1AJqu\" target=\"_blank\">Davao del Sur</a> and <a href=\"https://goo.gl/maps/1MUNyuPCKxD2\" target=\"_blank\">Surigao del Norte</a> (all Mindanao), <a href=\"https://goo.gl/maps/oqAwojWijYM2\" target=\"_blank\">Samar</a> (Eastern Visayas region), <a href=\"https://goo.gl/maps/ezjqZs5DpzM2\" target=\"_blank\">Negros Occidental</a> (Western Visayas region) and <a href=\"https://goo.gl/maps/PjNFi2MUoPu\" target=\"_blank\">Bicol region</a> (Luzon). Members travelling to or operating in these areas should remain vigilant and take stringent precautions. </p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>For travel in the aforementioned areas where the NPA is active, journey management plans should be in place ensuring that time and association with military, police and government personnel and infrastructure is minimised.</li> <li>Avoid non-essential cross-country travel due to risks posed by banditry, by poor driving standards and road conditions, and by militant activity. When undertaking essential road travel, do not self-drive; use an experienced and trusted local driver and ensure your vehicle is in good condition. Take precautions against carjacking and avoid high-risk areas.</li> <li>Where travel is linked to organisations in the extractive sector, personnel should ensure that their itinerary is supported by the company's security protocols and infrastructure. Mine infrastructure has been targeted; time on site should be minimised as far as is possible.</li> <li>The above advice is not exhaustive; monitor our alerts on the Philippines for notable developments and contact the nearest Assistance Centre for profile- and itinerary-specific advice prior to travel. </li>\n</ul>\n<ul> </ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The president cancelled the peace talks and government ceasefire on 4 February following a series of NPA rebel attacks on security forces and installations. Beginning on 29 January, the most notable of these attacks were in Isabela province (Luzon island) and Agusan del Norte, Cotabato, Sarangani and Surigao del Norte provinces (all Mindanao island). The NPA had announced the termination of a five-month ceasefire of its own on 1 February, claiming delays to the release of political prisoners, among other issues.</p>\n<p>Ceasefires between the CPP-NPA and the government have been periodically called and terminated since the late 1980s, with both sides trading accusations of truce violations. Hostilities often resume after a ceasefire ends, meaning sporadic attacks by the NPA can be expected. NPA activity largely consists of a well-established pattern of hit-and-run incidents, entailing clashes between the rebels and the security forces. The generally rural location of such incidents and the short-lived duration of individual confrontations mean that they pose only a limited threat to business travellers. </p>\n<p>NPA operations can also include raids against plantations, mining compounds and factories that refuse to meet extortion demands. Although the rebel group refers to these demands as a 'revolutionary tax', such raids are largely driven by criminal motives. They tend to involve acts of arson and vandalism of equipment rather than targeting personnel. However, employees based at targeted facilities are occasionally exposed to risks. </p>\n<p><em>Security measures</em></p>\n<p>Heightened security measures, including increased military patrols and vehicle checks, should be expected, particularly in the aforementioned areas where the NPA is active. Extra security personnel are likely to be deployed along routes in insurgency-affected areas, including the Pan-Philippine Highway, potentially causing minor delays to road travel. </p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>South-western Mindanao </b> - Philippines;  <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Kidnap<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>The authorities on 27 February confirmed that a German national, kidnapped in November 2016 while sailing off <a href=\"https://goo.gl/maps/sZqYcdKVDuk\" target=\"_blank\">Tawi-Tawi</a> province (Sulu archipelago, Mindanao island), had been beheaded by the militant Abu Sayyaf Group (ASG). The execution followed the expiry of a deadline set by the ASG for ransom payment. Kidnapping for ransom remains a persistent threat in southern and western parts of Mindanao, where the travel risks are rated HIGH. Essential travel to this region should only be undertaken after seeking itinerary-specific advice and with security support; travel to ASG's strongholds in the Sulu archipelago should be avoided.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>While travel to most of Mindanao is viable, seek itinerary- and profile-specific advice before travelling to the island. Travel to south-western Mindanao should be for essential purposes only. Avoid all travel to the Sulu archipelago due to the risks posed by insurgent and associated criminal activity.</li> <li>Trips to coastal resorts and islands should be arranged through reputable tour operators and include comprehensive itinerary and accommodation details. Travellers should select accommodation based on thorough research into and professional advice on security infrastructure, facilities, location and access to support. At a minimum, travellers should select guest rooms that do not have direct access to the waterfront.</li> <li>Criminals, including kidnappers, are commonly armed; if targeted, avoid doing anything to resist or antagonise your assailant.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on the Philippines for notable developments. </li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The police said the victim had been killed in the Indanan area of Sulu province (Sulu archipelago), where the ASG is currently holding several others, including foreigners, hostage. The body of the victim's female companion had been found in their abandoned yacht in Sulu in November; reports indicated she had been killed after resisting her captors. </p>\n<p>Abductions, mainly attributed to the ASG, pose a persistent threat to travellers; the threat is highest in the group's strongholds in Basilan and Jolo islands (both Sulu archipelago) and coastal or rural areas of southern and western Mindanao, namely Cotabato, Lanaodel Norte, Lanao del Sur, Maguindanao, Sultan Kudarat provinces and the Zamboanga Peninsula. While foreigners, especially those with Filipino relatives or living within Filipino communities, have been targeted for kidnapping, affluent locals, especially members of the ethnic Filipino-Chinese community, are usually the main targets. </p>\n<em>\n<p>Background</p>\n</em>\n<p>The latest abduction is at least the second such incident involving German nationals. In April 2015, ASG abducted two German nationals from their yacht off the coast of Bataraza (Palawan province, Luzon island); the victims spent six months in captivity and were released only after a ransom was paid. </p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Kidnap for ransom is a common fundraising tactic of semi-autonomous criminal gangs that now largely comprise the ASG. Foreigners are considered prime targets as they command high ransom payments. Abductions have primarily occurred in eastern coastal areas of Sabah state (Malaysia); however, significantly increased security measures along the Sabah coast have limited the ASG's ability to carry out further successful raids in the area.</p>\n<p>Since 2016, the ASG has increasingly turned to the kidnapping of local and foreign tugboat crew, usually Indonesian and Malaysian nationals, operating in international waters off the Sulu archipelago. Such incidents have occurred despite ongoing military offensives against the ASG in its strongholds, as well as co-ordination between the navies of the Philippines, Malaysia, and Indonesia in patrolling their sea borders. This illustrates the intent and capability of the ASG to carry out operations – including those targeting foreigners – in the Sulu Sea, as well as along the vulnerable southern and western coasts of Mindanao.</p>\n<p>There are concerns that ASG's pledges of support for the Iraq- and Syria-based Islamic State (IS) extremist group help drive abductions and beheadings of foreign hostages. It is possible that Mindanao-based Islamists are emulating killings of captive foreigners by other militant groups to gain greater credibility within the wider global jihadist network and exert pressure on subsequent ransom negotiations. However, beheadings are likely to remain isolated and the ASG's focus will continue to be on lucrative abductions; it should be noted that the release of Indonesian and Malaysian hostages in 2016 likely took place following payment of ransom.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "created": "2017-02-06T06:54:30Z",
          "modified": "2017-03-06T06:36:29Z",
          "categories": [
            {
              "primary": "Medical Alert ",
              "sub": " Disease Outbreak"

          ],
          "long": 45.07916,
          "lat": 23.88594,
          "id": 952617,
          "version": 2,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Saudi Arabia; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Coronavirus<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Several cases of Middle East Respiratory syndrome (MERS-CoV) have been recorded in Saudi Arabia since the start of this year, including some fatalities. There is no suggestion that the virus is transmitting more readily. Overall the risk to travellers is low if precautions are taken. Avoid contact with sick people and practice good general hygiene. Avoid contact with camels and do not eat or drink raw camel products, this is especially important if you have underlying health problems. If you require medical attention, call International SOS and we will arrange an appropriate referral. International SOS is monitoring the situation closely.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:\n</p>\n<ul> <li>Avoid contact with sick people, including in healthcare facilities.</li> <li>Avoid contact with live animals, especially camels, and their environments such as farms, markets or barns.</li> <li>Pay attention to general hygiene:<br/> <ul> <li>Wash your hands frequently.</li> <li>Avoid touching your face.</li> <li>Keep some distance from people who are coughing, sneezing or appear sick.</li> <li>Ensure any food or drink for consumption is safe - thoroughly washed, peeled, boiled, cooked through etc. Do not eat or drink raw camel products including raw camel milk, camel urine and undercooked meat.</li> </ul> </li> <li>Should you require medical attention, call any assistance center and we will arrange an appropriate referral.</li> <li>Anyone who develops a fever and respiratory symptoms (such as cough) which are more than mild should seek medical attention and mention their travel history. You may also be required to disclose information about your medical and travel history when visiting other countries.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>In February 2017, 18 cases of MERS-CoV were recorded. Affected places include Alasyah, Al Makhwah, Alzulfi, Bisha, Hail, Hafr Albatin, Jeddah, Khaybar, Makkah, Madinah, Muhayl, Najran and Riyadh. Most of the cases are primary and some have underlying health problems. Contact tracing is underway.</p>\n<p>Some people who have tested positive for the virus have no symptoms, some have had a mild illness, while others are critically ill, and fatalities continue to be reported. The risk of severe illness is higher among people with chronic diseases or a weakened immune system. Overall the risk has not changed. While the virus can be transmitted from one person to another, this has generally occurred between close contacts in households, and in healthcare facilities.</p>\n<p>\nWhat is MERS-CoV?\n</p>\n<p>Middle East respiratory syndrome coronavirus (MERS-CoV), known previously as \"novel coronavirus\", was first identified in humans in 2012. It is from the same family of viruses as SARS (Severe Acute Respiratory Syndrome), the coronavirus family. Most cases have originated from the Middle East, although a large outbreak associated with hospitals occurred in South Korea in 2015. While some people may have no symptoms at all, others may have a fever and cough. Some, particularly those with underlying health issues, progress to a severe disease with pneumonia and respiratory failure. About 30-40% of cases are fatal. There is no specific treatment and no vaccine available.\n</p>\n<p>Direct contact with dromedary camels, consuming undercooked camel meat, or drinking raw camel products (milk, urine etc) are risk factors for infection. Spread can occur from one person to others in close contact, possibly through infected respiratory droplets. Outbreaks and clusters of infection have occurred in the healthcare environment and within household.</p>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See the dedicated MERS-CoV pages of the International SOS Pandemic Preparedness website</li> <li>About MERS-CoV: see the article \"coronavirus\" on the Disease and Prevention dropdown on the International SOS country guides</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Sporadic cases are likely to continue. Cases may be exported by travellers from Saudi Arabia to other countries. Clusters of infection may occur within healthcare settings if appropriate infection control practices are not implemented as soon as patients enter facilities.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>\nIf relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of:</p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including MERS-CoV, who is most at risk (especially people who have pre-existing illnesses and healthcare workers), and prevention strategies. </li> <li>Who to contact for advice and treatment if they get sick during or after travel.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "created": "2016-08-27T15:46:46Z",
          "modified": "2017-02-13T06:43:51Z",

          "long": 103.8501,

          "id": 948690,
          "version": 19,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Singapore; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Zika virus<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Local Zika cases have been reported in Singapore. The outbreak was centered around the Aljunied Crescent, Sims Drive, Kallang Way and Payar Lebar areas, however some confirmed cases worked or live in other areas of the city. The first local cases were detected in August 2016. The virus may now be established in the local mosquito population. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. While less common, sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms are usually mild and self-limited, severe and irreversible birth defects may occur in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women should consider delaying travel to Zika-affected areas. Those who are in affected areas should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission. After travel to the affected areas, ongoing precautions against sexual transmission are recommended.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel. Many health authorities including the United States CDC and the World Health Organization advise pregnant women not to travel to areas with active transmission of Zika virus. </p>\n<p>For those who will travel to, or are in Singapore: </p>\n<ul> <li>Prevent infection by preventing mosquito bites: Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535. When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks). Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Consider using an insecticide-treated bed net. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room. Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible. </li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission through the use of condoms (or abstinence). </li>\n</ul>\nAfter Travel:\n<ul> <li>Pregnant women - Consult your doctor. You may be advised to be tested for Zika whether or not you develop symptoms. Your doctor may also arrange additional monitoring or specialist care. </li> <li>All travellers - Monitor your health for two weeks. If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor and advise them of your travel history. This is especially important if you are, or are trying to fall pregnant. </li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission - Anyone who has a pregnant partner should use condoms (applicable to males and females) or abstain for the duration of the pregnancy. For those whose partners are not pregnant, authorities differ in their recommendations for the duration for which precautions should continue. <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">The World Health Organization advises</a> that everyone should continue precautions for at least 6 months after return. <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/prevention/protect-yourself-during-sex.html\" target=\"_blank\">The United States CDC advises</a>: <strong>Men</strong> should continue precautions for at least 6 months; <strong>Women </strong>to continue precautions for at least 8 weeks. </li> <li>Consider delaying pregnancy - The World Health Organization <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> couples or women planning a pregnancy, who have returned from an area with Zika transmission, to wait at least 6 months. The United States CDC <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/pregnancy/women-and-their-partners.html\" target=\"_blank\">recommend</a> women trying to get pregnant wait at least 8 weeks and partners of women trying to get pregnant wait at least 6 months. </li> <li>Prevent transmission to local mosquitoes. If you are returning to an area that has mosquitoes which can transmit Zika, continue to prevent mosquito bites for 2 to 3 weeks (e.g. using insect repellent). This will reduce the risk of infecting local mosquitoes with Zika, and therefore reduce the risk of an outbreak.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>A number of authorities, including the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, have advised pregnant women to avoid travel to Singapore due to the Zika outbreak. Over 350 people have been confirmed to have acquired the infection locally via mosquito bites. Investigations are ongoing and health authorities have screened close contacts of confirmed cases as well as others who have had symptoms and may be potentially infected. An intensive awareness and mosquito control campaign is underway.</p>\n<p>What is Zika?</p>\n<p>Zika is a viral disease, mostly transmitted to people by mosquito bites. Zika virus can be passed from an infected pregnant mother to her baby. Spread through sexual contact occurs. Transmission via blood transfusion has been reported. Most people who have Zika infections do not have any symptoms. If symptoms occur, they are similar to chikungunya or dengue, and include fever, rash, joint and muscle pains or conjunctivitis (red eyes), and typically last up to a week. The virus can cause severe irreversible birth defects, such as \"microcephaly\" (a small head with developmental abnormalities), and serious neurological complications. There is no specific treatment available, and no vaccine to prevent the disease. </p>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See the <a href=\"http://mailgun.internationalsos.com/c/eJxtjktvgzAQhH8N3Iy8xs-DDxCgQmpOSL1vwQTU8JDtSGl_fX2NlNXcdma-mSx3MM75sEY3Ht710W1fzof12G2ZL3bSoDXOEwJwBXQ2JQJLZ3QpkEmW3-0S4xmysspYl3TiPrltHYt1j87vGFMT3sMRivHY0v9v_cEX2ifutwfenHV7vtqKatWqriaN5kC4EkA0ACVtKaSsWVMJZjJOfXiEsMXzLSX39vT4vWDxxCcm91vT8BsSvZ8sf5nTNzZTtbx0AroGCOXQEW7EhRijDVG1YVK3SknRZKrJP67D1YWQ5qcco1wryv4BncFq3Q\" target=\"_blank\">International SOS Zika website</a></li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on Singapore</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>The virus is likely present in the local mosquito population and further human cases may occur.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of: <ul> <li>Regional health threats including Zika, who is most at risk (such as pregnant women), and prevention strategies. </li> <li>Who to contact for advice and treatment if they get sick during or after travel. </li> <li>How to access bite prevention resources including insect repellent and bed nets. </li>\n</ul>\nConsider: <ul> <li>Providing a travel health kit, including insect repellent. </li> <li>Stipulating a medical pre-travel consultation.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Suspected militants threw grenades at the building, with gunfire reported afterwards. While no group has so far claimed responsibility, the incident illustrates the continued presence and growing intent of militant factions to carry out attacks within Bosaso. Members should continue to defer all non-essential travel to Puntland; those currently in Bosaso should avoid the vicinity of the targeted court building and follow all directives issued by the authorities.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Continue to avoid the vicinity of the regional courthouse in Bosaso and follow all directives issued by the authorities. Liaise with your local security provider regarding the feasibility of movement outside Bosaso. </li> <li>Essential travel to most of Puntland, where the travel risks are rated as HIGH, requires stringent security precautions, including a specific pre-travel threat assessment as part of a comprehensive and fully co-ordinated security programme. Travel to the Galgala mountains and coastal areas east of Bosaso should be deferred. </li> <li>Travellers on long-term assignments should receive hostile environment training prior to deployment. Seek professional 24/7 security support to ensure access to the latest locally sourced threat intelligence, trip-specific assessments, operations-room tracking, reliable communications and immediate access to emergency support. </li> <li>Minimise movement outside secure premises and the time spent around likely targets for militant attacks, both static and in transit. </li> <li>Monitor our Somalia alerts for further information.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The incident follows a 26 February grenade attack on a checkpoint in Bosaso, in which four soldiers were injured. While the al-Shabab organisation – some of whose members have reportedly been tried at the targeted court – remains the primary Islamist extremist group in Somalia, a pro-Islamic State (IS) faction has recently stepped up its activity in Puntland, killing four security guards in an 8 February attack on the International Village Hotel in Bosaso.</p> <p>The incidence of militancy in Puntland remains generally lower than in southern and central Somalia. However, the recent developments indicate such groups' growing intent to target northern urban centres outside traditional militant strongholds.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Al-Shabab's capabilities remain far greater than the recently established pro-IS faction. Nevertheless, the presence of two separate groups, possibly competing for territorial dominance, suggests that the militant threat in Puntland may increase in the coming months.</p> <p>This will be exacerbated by the region's financial limitations, overstretched resources and the diminishing capability and poor morale of its security forces, some of whose members on 26 February briefly seized the Puntland parliament building in the regional capital Garowe to demand better working conditions and overdue salaries. Given the above considerations, as well as the lack of secure accommodation options and access to assistance in the territory, we are currently reviewing Puntland's travel risk rating.</p> </p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

While the majority of cases have been reported from districts along the Shebelle River, an ongoing drought has exacerbated the spread of cases into the south central and north-eastern areas. This is an ongoing outbreak that began in 2016. Cholera is consistently present in the country. It spreads via contaminated food and water. Symptoms include vomiting and profuse, watery diarrhoea which can lead to severe dehydration. Travellers staying in quality accommodation are at lower risk. Pay strict attention to hygiene and consider vaccination.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:\n</p>\n<ul> <li>If available, vaccination is recommended for travellers and relief workers who are likely to encounter unsanitary conditions, or will have limited access to safe water.</li> <li>Drink only boiled or bottled water, water that has been treated with chlorine or iodine, or carbonated beverages.</li> <li>Select food, water and other beverages carefully.</li> <li>Choose food that has been thoroughly cooked while fresh and is served hot.</li> <li>Avoid ice, raw fruit, and raw vegetables (including salad). Fruit and vegetables that you peel yourself are safer choices.</li> <li>Pay close attention to hygiene. Wash your hands frequently, especially before eating.</li> <li>Get medical advice / treatment if you develop severe diarrhoea and/or vomiting.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Consider:</p>\n<ul> <li>Carrying water purification supplies with you.</li>\n</ul>\n<ul>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Since the beginning of January 2017, more than 7,700 suspected cholera cases have been reported including at least 180 deaths. This corresponds to a case-fatality rate of around 2.4%. Heavily affected areas include Bay, Banaadir, Shabeellaha Dhexe (Middle Shabelle) and Shabeellaha Hoose (Lower Shabelle) regions along the Shebelle River. As the drought continues, the outbreak has spread further with cases now reported in Bakool, Bari, Gedo, Hiiran, Lower Juba, Mudug and Nugal regions.</p>\n<p>Health authorities are working to contain the outbreak by setting up treatment centres, treating water sources and distributing hygiene kits.</p>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See \"Cholera\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>See the Travel Advice article \"Selecting Safe Food and Water\" on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on Somalia.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/march-2017/somalia-cholera-7-mar-2017.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

During the latest related rallies held on 1 March, thousands of pro-and anti-Park protesters gathered at Gwanghwamun Square amid a heightened security presence. The demonstrations passed off peacefully. <br/></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Avoid all demonstrations as a precaution and to minimise travel delays. </li> <li>If travel in the vicinity of protest locations is unavoidable, maintain flexible itineraries and allow additional time for important journeys. </li> <li>Monitor our alerts on South Korea for further related developments, including the dates of Constitutional Court hearings in the case.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p>According to the organisers, around 300,000 participated in the anti-Park demonstration at Gwanghwamun Square on 1 March. As the special prosecutors' team to investigate the graft scandal was denied permission to extend their probe, a significant number of people are expected to gather in the coming protests to demand further investigation. Most related gatherings will be held amid tight security, which, along with the large crowds, will cause significant traffic disruption.<br/></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Park has been under pressure to resign following her admission that she gave an old friend, Choi Soon-sil, inappropriate access to classified government documents and received unofficial assistance on state affairs from Choi. Among other charges, Park is accused of colluding with her friend to coerce major conglomerates into donating funds to foundations set up by Choi.</p>\nPark's previous apologies, reshuffle of senior officials and promise to devolve some of her executive powers to a prime minister chosen by parliament all failed to stem the fallout from the scandal, which culminated in a successful impeachment vote. Protesters are demanding a swift verdict from the Constitutional Court, as well as the resignation of prime minister and acting president Hwang Kyo-ahn. The court is expected to issue its verdict on <strong>13 March </strong>on whether Park should be removed from office or reinstated. <br/></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/tsalertmaps/2017/february/south-korea-seoul-protest-20170201.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>South Sudan; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Cholera<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>The cholera outbreak continues across the country, with Juba in Jubek State recording the most cases. Other affected states include Eastern Lakes, Eastern Nile, Imatong, Jonglei, Northern Liech, Southern Liech, Terekeka, and Western Bieh. Cholera is consistently present in the country. It spreads via contaminated food and water. Symptoms include vomiting and profuse, watery diarrhoea which can lead to severe dehydration. Travellers staying in quality accommodation are at lower risk. Pay strict attention to hygiene and consider vaccination.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p>Do the following:<br/>\n<ul> <li>If available, vaccination is recommended for travellers and relief workers who are likely to encounter unsanitary conditions, or will have limited access to safe water.</li> <li>Drink only boiled or bottled water, water that has been treated with chlorine or iodine, or carbonated beverages.</li> <li>Select food, water and other beverages carefully.</li> <li>Choose food that has been thoroughly cooked while fresh and is served hot.</li> <li>Avoid ice, raw fruit, and raw vegetables (including salad). Fruit and vegetables that you peel yourself are safer choices.</li> <li>Pay close attention to hygiene. Wash your hands frequently, especially before eating.</li> <li>Get medical advice / treatment if you develop severe diarrhoea and/or vomiting.</li>\n</ul>\nConsider:\n<ul> <li>Carrying water purification supplies with you.</li>\n</ul>\n<ul>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Active transmission continues in at least nine states. More than 5,300 cases have been reported with at least 122 deaths. The World Health Organization and other agencies are responding to the ongoing outbreak, managing cases and supporting Water, Sanitation and Health (WASH) activities to reduce the spread of the disease. Vaccination programmes are also required to improve the rate of cholera immunity, particularly in Bentiu, Northern Liech.</p>\n<p>For further information:<br/>\n<ul> <li>See \"Cholera\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>See the Travel Advice article \"Selecting Safe Food and Water\" on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>About South Sudan: See the International SOS Country Guide.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul>\n</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Children aged between 5 and 14 are most affected, most of whom have not been fully vaccinated. Measles is caused by a highly contagious virus that spreads from person-to-person via infected droplets.           "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>South Sudan; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Measles<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Measles cases continue to be reported. Wau is heavily affected, other areas reporting cases include; the cities of Bor and Yambio, Western Bahr el Ghazal, Gogrial West in Warrap, and Aweil South in Northern Bahr el Ghazal. Children aged between 5 and 14 are most affected, most of whom have not been fully vaccinated. Measles is caused by a highly contagious virus that spreads from person-to-person via infected droplets. Outbreaks occur frequently in under-vaccinated populations. Common symptoms include fever, cough and a characteristic rash. The illness can result in serious complications, particularly when very young children, the elderly or pregnant women are infected. All travellers should ensure they are fully immunised against measles.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<ul> <li>Ensure you are immune to measles before travelling. Get vaccinated if you haven't had measles in the past or if you haven't had two doses of measles vaccine.</li> <li>Keep young children and other people who are not immune to measles away from outbreak areas.</li> <li>If you are unsure about your immunity and may have been exposed, or if you develop symptoms, seek medical attention. Call before visiting in person, so staff can protect themselves and other patients from this very contagious virus.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>About measles: See \"measles\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on South Sudan.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/march-2017/south-sudan-measles-mar-2017.jpg\"/></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>South Sudan; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Conflict<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Members should continue to avoid all non-essential travel to the capital Juba, and travel to most other regions of South Sudan, as the country's crisis persists. A new rebel group was announced on 6 March by a former army general, Lieutenant General Thomas Cirillo Swaka; his National Salvation Front opposes President Salva Kiir's continued rule. The development demonstrates the increasing fragmentation of the conflict and lowers the likelihood of an improvement in security conditions.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Business-critical travel to Juba, a HIGH travel risk destination, is possible with enhanced security precautions. A full itinerary-specific briefing on the prevailing threats is necessary prior to travel, with pre-arranged transport and secure accommodation and a robust and resilient local assistance network (logistical, security as well as medical).</li> <li>Members in the capital should maintain the ability to stand fast for periods up to at least seven days in the event of a prolonged airport closure.</li> <li>All travel should be avoided to other parts of South Sudan, with the exception of the better-protected Palouge oil fields in Upper Nile state, where travel by air is possible on a case-by-case basis subject to itinerary-specific pre-travel threat assessment.</li> <li>Members in areas outside the capital should relocate to Juba when it is practical to do so in a sufficiently secure manner. Travel should be conducted by air, where possible.</li> <li>The above advice is not exhaustive; see our Standing Travel Advice on South Sudan for further details.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Swaka was one of five high-profile members of the government or military who quit in February citing dissatisfaction with Kiir's leadership and the dominance in power of his Dinka community. Two of these defectors joined the opposition Sudan People's Liberation Movement-In Opposition (SPLM-IO). The resignations are likely to prompt retaliatory military activity in the north and Equatoria region. </p>\nThe SPLM-IO, too, has been split since Kiir sacked SPLM-IO leader Riek Machar as First Vice President in July 2016 and replaced him with another SPLM-IO official, Taban Deng Gai. The fragmentation and proliferation of rebel groups, particularly in the Greater Equatoria region, means localised violence is likely to continue.<br/></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "summary": "<![CDATA[An outbreak of hepatitis A has been reported in Barcelona. The majority affected are men who have sex with men, and there is no suggestion of a food-borne outbreak. Spain generally has seen a rise in the number of hepatitis A cases in this population, and several people were diagnosed with the disease after travelling to Spain. Hepatitis A is a viral infection of the liver, spread through the \"fecal-oral\" route, via direct person-to-person contact or contaminated food and water. Symptoms can take a month to appear and include fever, fatigue, abdominal pain, nausea, vomiting, dark urine and jaundice (yellow colour of the skin and eyes). Prevention is through vaccination and attention to hygiene.]]>",
          "created": "2017-02-24T04:49:07Z",

          "long": 2.080456,

          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Barcelona </b> - Spain;  <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Hepatitis A<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>An outbreak of hepatitis A has been reported in Barcelona. The majority affected are men who have sex with men, and there is no suggestion of a food-borne outbreak. Spain generally has seen a rise in the number of hepatitis A cases in this population, and several people were diagnosed with the disease after travelling to Spain. Hepatitis A is a viral infection of the liver, spread through the \"fecal-oral\" route, via direct person-to-person contact or contaminated food and water. Symptoms can take a month to appear and include fever, fatigue, abdominal pain, nausea, vomiting, dark urine and jaundice (yellow colour of the skin and eyes). Prevention is through vaccination and attention to hygiene.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<ul> <li>If you have not previously been vaccinated against hepatitis A, see your doctor before departure and consider vaccination, especially if you are in the risk group. </li> <li>Maintain a high level of personal hygiene, including during sexual activity. </li> <li>Wash hands frequently, including before food preparation.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See \"Hepatitis A\" in the diseases and prevention dropdown on the International SOS country guides.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Sri Lanka; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Dengue fever<br />\n<b><i>Event Date</i></b>: 04 Feb 2017</p>\n<p><p>An increase in cases of dengue fever has been reported across the country, with the Western Province being largely affected. Although dengue is persistently present in Sri Lanka, the activity this year is higher than for the same period in 2016. The disease is spread by mosquitoes and is present in both rural and urban or city areas. Dengue can cause a range of symptoms and has no particular treatment. Some people, especially those who have been infected before, get a more severe form of disease that can lead to fatal complications. Prevent infection by avoiding mosquito bites</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<ul> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks).</li> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535.</li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room.</li> <li>Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.</li> <li>Seek medical attention if you develop a high fever, especially if you suffer \"rigors\" (shaking) or a rash.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>About dengue: see \"Dengue Fever\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>About preventing mosquito bites: see the Travel Advice articles \"Preventing Mosquito Bites\" and \"Using Insect Repellents Safely\" on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on Sri Lanka.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

According to the authorities, reported crime in the country increased to 1,643 in 2016 compared to 1,048 in 2015. The latest official figure serves as a reminder of the need for members in the country to adopt sensible precautions at all times to reduce the risk of exposure to crime. <br/></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to St Kitts and Nevis can continue with sensible precautions. </li> <li>Petty crime represents the main risk to travellers. Remain vigilant at all times; care should be taken when walking off main roads and after dark. </li> <li>Incidents of burglary, armed robbery and assault have been known to take place, in the major resort areas. Do not leave valuables unattended in public areas or unsecured hotel rooms.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on St Kitts and Nevis for further updates.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The latest figures show a marked increase in crimes related to shootings, arson and theft of personal property. Homicides rates increased marginally: 31 incidents were reported in 2016 compared to 28 in the previous year. While personal property theft increased by 71% in 2016 compared to 2015, robberies decreased by 12% over the same period. Despite the increase in crime nationwide, the overall security environment in St Kitts and Nevis remains stable, and the increase in crime is not expected to result in any deterioration of the travel security situation. </p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

HFMD is caused by a virus and is usually a relatively mild illness. Symptoms include a fever, sores in the mouth and a red rash on the hands and feet that may blister. The illness spreads from person-to-person and occasionally it can be severe. It mostly affects children under the age of five. Prevent infection by paying attention to hygiene.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p> <ul> <li>Wash your hands frequently and thoroughly with soap and water - especially after using the toilet or changing a diaper.</li> <li>Do not share food, drinks or eating utensils.</li> <li>Avoid close contact, such as kissing and hugging.</li> <li>Disinfect contaminated surfaces and items by washing with a diluted chlorine-containing product or an iodized disinfectant.</li> </ul> <p>If someone has HFMD symptoms, they should: </p> <ul> <li>Stay at home. </li> <li>Ensure adequate fluid intake. </li> <li>Seek medical attention if the rash is accompanied by a persistent high fever, vomiting or a seizure.</li> </ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>So far, there have been over 7,500 cases, most of them children. The most affected provinces include Chanthaburi, Chiang Rai, Lampang, Phuket and Surat Thani. </p> <p> HFMD activity in Thailand is present throughout the year and seasonal outbreaks are common.</p> <p>For More Information: </p> <ul> <li>See the Disease and Prevention article \"Hand Foot and Mouth Disease\" on the International SOS Country Guides </li> <li>About Thailand: see the International SOS Country Guide </li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre</li> </ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

At least four people were killed in an ambush by militants on a pickup truck carrying family members of a local government official in Rueso district (Narathiwat). In Pattani's Mayo district, at least four people – three soldiers and a security guard – were killed and two civilians injured in two separate attacks. <br/></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to Narathiwat, Pattani and Yala provinces, as well as the southern districts (Chana, Na Thawi, Saba Yoi and Thepha) of Songkhla province, should be for essential purposes only due to risks posed by the ongoing insurgency.</li> <li>Travellers intending to visit the region should seek itinerary-specific advice and ensure that they have mitigating measures in place prior to travel, including journey management planning, communications protocols and consideration of formal security support. Particular caution is required in the selection of accommodation; overnight stays should be avoided where possible.</li> <li>Rail travel along the south-eastern branch of the southern railway from Hat Yai (Songkhla) to Sungai Kolok (Narathiwat) should be avoided, as separatist insurgents have previously attacked the track, rolling stock and stations on this line.</li> <li>Reduce your exposure to potential targets for militant attacks, which primarily include security force personnel and convoys, checkpoints, police stations, government buildings, schools, religious sites. Remain vigilant at all times; report any suspicious behaviour or suspect packages to the authorities.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on Thailand for related updates.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p>The incidents came two days after peace talks held in Malaysia between the government and the militant umbrella organisation MARA Patani, during which both sides agreed to establish safety zones in five districts in the southern provinces. While MARA Patani claims to represent the main militant groups, it does not control all insurgent groups on the ground, including the Barisan Revolusi Nasional (BRN) – the region's most capable militant organisation. Groups not aligned with MARA Patani have launched attacks in the past to demonstrate their disagreement with peace talks. </p>\n<p>The majority of separatist activity will continue to be directed at the security forces, with other common targets including farmers, Buddhist monks and teachers. However, the targeting pattern of militant groups has increasingly involved indiscriminately attacking civilians with explosives. Co-ordinated bombings during the 11-12 August 2016 holiday marking Queen Sirikit's birthday killed four civilians and injured more than 20 others, including ten foreigners. There is a continued potential for occasional attacks on 'soft' targets such as hotels, schools and crowded marketplaces in regional urban centres. Attacks against transport infrastructure, particularly trains running between Yala, Pattani and Narathiwat, occur occasionally.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p>Prospects for the success of the peace talks remain poor, which is likely to exacerbate disagreements between various insurgent factions and prompt further attacks. Previously, efforts by the government and MARA Patani to implement a ceasefire zone in Narathiwat resulted in militants seizing a civilian hospital in March 2016. Militants have typically launched attacks around significant dates and events to signal their intentions and demonstrate capability. Trends suggest the potential for militant attacks outside of the southern provinces around key dates and sensitive anniversaries.</p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "title": "<![CDATA[Zika virus]]>",
          "summary": "<![CDATA[Locally-acquired Zika cases have been reported from a number of regions including Bangkok, BuengKan, Chiang Mai, Chanthaburi, and Phetchabun. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. While less common, sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms are usually mild and self-limited, severe and irreversible birth defects may occur in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider delaying travel to Zika-affected areas. Those who are in affected areas should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission.           "created": "2016-09-01T00:30:11Z",
          "modified": "2017-02-20T11:14:12Z",
          "categories": [
            {
              "primary": "Medical Alert ",
              "sub": " Disease Outbreak"
            }
          ],
          "long": 101,
          "lat": 15.5,
          "id": 948798,
          "version": 23,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Thailand; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Zika virus<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Locally-acquired Zika cases have been reported from a number of regions including Bangkok, BuengKan, Chiang Mai, Chanthaburi, and Phetchabun. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. While less common, sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms are usually mild and self-limited, severe and irreversible birth defects may occur in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider delaying travel to Zika-affected areas. Those who are in affected areas should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission. After travel to the affected areas, ongoing precautions against sexual transmission are recommended.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel. Many health authorities advise pregnant women not to travel to areas with active transmission of Zika virus. </p>\n<p>For those who will travel to, or are in Thailand:</p>\n<ul> <li>Prevent infection by preventing mosquito bites: Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535. When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks). Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Consider using an insecticide-treated bed net. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room. Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible. </li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission through the use of condoms (or abstinence).</li>\n</ul>\n<p>After travel:</p>\n<ul> <li>Pregnant women - Consult your doctor. You may be advised to be tested for Zika whether or not you develop symptoms. Your doctor may also arrange additional monitoring or specialist care.</li> <li>All travellers - Monitor your health for two weeks. If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor and advise them of your travel history. This is especially important if you are, or are trying to fall pregnant. </li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission - Anyone who has a pregnant partner should use condoms (applicable to males and females) or abstain for the duration of the pregnancy. For those whose partners are not pregnant, authorities differ in their recommendations for the duration for which precautions should continue. <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">The World Health Organization advises</a> that everyone should continue precautions for at least 6 months after return. <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/prevention/protect-yourself-during-sex.html\" target=\"_blank\">The United States CDC advises</a>: <strong>Men</strong> should continue precautions for at least 6 months; <strong>Women</strong> to continue precautions for at least 8 weeks</li> <li>Consider delaying pregnancy - The World Health Organization <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> couples or women planning a pregnancy, who have returned from an area with Zika transmission, to wait at least 6 months. The United States CDC <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/pregnancy/women-and-their-partners.html\" target=\"_blank\">recommends</a> women trying to get pregnant wait at least 8 weeks and partners of women trying to get pregnant wait at least 6 months.</li> <li>Prevent transmission to local mosquitoes. If you are returning to an area that has mosquitoes which can transmit Zika, continue to prevent mosquito bites for 2 to 3 weeks (e.g. using insect repellent). This will reduce the risk of infecting local mosquitoes with Zika, and therefore reduce the risk of an outbreak.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The first locally-transmitted cases of Zika virus were reported in 2012. Sporadic cases have been documented since then, including in travellers after they left Thailand. In 2016, over 350 local cases were recorded from at least 15 provinces, including of Bangkok, Bueng karn, Chanthaburi, Chiangmai and Phetchaboon. </p>\n<p>What is Zika?</p>\n<p>Zika is a viral disease, mostly transmitted to people by mosquito bites. Zika virus can be passed from an infected pregnant mother to her baby. Spread through sexual contact occurs. Transmission via blood transfusion has been reported. Most people who have Zika infections do not have any symptoms. If symptoms occur, they are similar to chikungunya or dengue, and include fever, rash, joint and muscle pains or conjunctivitis (red eyes), and typically last up to a week. The virus can cause severe irreversible birth defects, such as \"microcephaly\" (a small head with developmental abnormalities), and serious neurological complications. There is no specific treatment available, and no vaccine to prevent the disease.</p>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See the <a href=\"https://pandemic.internationalsos.com/zika\" target=\"_blank\">International SOS Zika website</a></li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide for Thailand</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>There is an ongoing risk of Zika in Thailand, further cases are likely to occur. </p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of\n</p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including Zika, who is most at risk (such as pregnant women), and prevention strategies. </li> <li>Who to contact for advice and treatment if they get sick during or after travel. </li> <li>How to access bite prevention resources including insect repellent and bed nets. </li>\n</ul>\n<p>Consider:</p>\n<ul> <li>Providing a travel health kit, including insect repellent. </li> <li>Stipulating a medical pre-travel consultation.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/october-2016/thai-zika-map.jpg\"/></p>]]>"
        }

          "created": "2017-02-24T07:00:43Z",
          "modified": "2017-02-24T07:00:43Z",
          "categories": [
            {
              "primary": "Medical Alert ",
              "sub": " Disease Outbreak"

          ],
          "long": 1.042553,
          "lat": 8.766221,
          "id": 953079,
          "version": 1,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Togo; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Meningitis meningococcal<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Cases of suspected bacterial meningitis have been reported in several parts of the country. Meningococcal meningitis is a serious bacterial infection that can be rapidly life-threatening. Anyone can be affected, although infants, adolescents and college students are at higher risk. It spreads from person-to-person via infected droplets. Symptoms include fever, intense headache, vomiting, neck stiffness and coma. Severe cases develop a characteristic rash all over the body. It can be treated with antibiotics. A vaccine is available.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p>Do the following:\n<ul> <li>Consider receiving the quadrivalent meningitis vaccine (serogroups A, C, Y and W135) before arrival in Togo. See your travel health practitioner prior to departure for an individual recommendation.</li> <li>Maintain strict personal hygiene.</li> <li>Avoid crowds in outbreak areas.</li> <li>Do not have close contact (such as kissing) with potentially infected people.</li> <li>Wash your hands frequently whether you are sick or healthy.</li> <li>If you develop symptoms, seek immediate medical attention.</li>\n</ul>\n<br/>\n<ul>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Cases of suspected meningococcal meningitis have been reported since the beginning of the year. Some deaths have also occurred. Lab tests have confirmed Neisseria meningitis infection in a few patients. Surveillance has been strengthened and vaccination camps are being planned.</p>\nTogo lies in the African meningitis belt and cases and deaths occur almost every year. A large outbreak was recorded in 2016 as well.<br/>\n<br/>\nWhat is meningitis?<br/>\nThere are several forms of meningitis, viral and bacterial. Bacterial meningitis is caused by various pathogens including Nisseria meningitidis which causes meningococcal meningitis.<br/>\n<br/>\nMeningococcal meningitis is one of the most severe forms and often becomes fatal. People can contract this disease if they inhale droplets that have been coughed or sneezed into the air by an infected person. Direct contact with an infected person's throat secretions (e.g. through kissing, sharing drinks) can also spread the disease. Typically, epidemics of meningococcal meningitis occur among people who live in close quarters. The bacterium invades the brain and its linings and can multiply in the blood (septicaemia). A characteristic red, blotchy rash occurs all over the body in most serious cases. Symptoms include fever, intense headache, vomiting, and neck stiffness. These may progress to cause coma. It is essential that infected people are evaluated by a physician immediately and receive antibiotics quickly, as the disease progresses rapidly and can cause permanent brain damage or death. Vaccines against some strains of the disease are available.<br/>\n<br/>\nViral meningitis, also called aseptic meningitis, is an inflammation of the lining of the brain and spinal cord. It is usually caused by a group of viruses called enteroviruses. These are spread through the respiratory secretions and faeces of an infected person. The classic symptoms, which usually come on quickly, are fever, headache and stiff neck. These symptoms may often occur together with nausea, vomiting, photophobia (sensitivity to light) and altered mental status (confusion).<br/>\n<br/>\nThere is no vaccine for viral meningitis, and no specific treatment for it. Most people recover in about a week. There are no long term complications.<br/>\n<br/>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See the article \"Meningococcal meningitis\" in the \"Disease &amp; Prevention\" dropdown on International SOS Country Guides</li> <li>See Togo on the International SOS Country Guides</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre</li>\n</ul>\n<br/>\n</p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Meningitis is endemic (consistently present) in Togo. The risk increases during the dry season between December to June.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure:</p>\n<ul> <li>A consultation with travel health practitioner for an individual recommendation on vaccination</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Also ensure your employees or travellers are aware of:</p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including meningitis, who is most at-risk and prevention strategies.</li> <li>Who to contact for advice and treatment if they get sick during or after travel.</li>\n</ul>\n<p><br/>\n</p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Tunisia; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Terrorism<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>A nationwide state of emergency in place since November 2015 has again been extended, this time by three months, to <strong>16 May</strong>. The measure grants the security forces additional powers, including the ability to prevent protests, temporarily close entertainment venues and secure control of the press and other publications. There is a persistent threat of militancy in Tunisia, particularly in areas bordering Algeria and Libya. While travel to most parts of the country can proceed, members should remain vigilant at all times.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travel to most parts of Tunisia can proceed. </li> <li>Avoid all but essential travel to areas of Jendouba, Kasserine and Kef provinces within 25 miles (40km) of the Algerian border and to within 25 miles (40km) of the Libyan border due to the risks posed by militant activity. Travel to these areas should only be undertaken after liaising with the local authorities. </li> <li>Curfews can be imposed at short notice following a major security incident. Observe and follow all directives issued by the authorities. </li> <li>There is an underlying threat of attack by Islamist militants in Tunisia. Likely targets include Western embassies, tourism sites, venues where Westerners are known to congregate and government or military personnel and assets. Report any suspicious behaviour or packages to the authorities and follow all directives issued by the security forces. </li> <li>If applicable for your nationality, register with your embassy and relevant warden networks.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>President Beji Caid Essebsi announced the latest extension to the state of emergency on 16 February. Several factors contribute to the underlying terrorist threat, including Tunisia's geographical position between two countries with a known and entrenched Islamist militant presence (Algeria and Libya), as well as its Western-leaning and secularist tradition and the presence – albeit declining – of foreign tourists and expatriates.</p>\n<p>Security operations are regularly conducted and terrorist cells dismantled, most recently, according to reports on 11 February, in Monastir province, where six suspects were arrested in a security operation. According to the interior ministry, the suspects are accused of hiring militant fighters for the extremist Islamic State (IS) group in Syria.</p>\n<p>In Tunisia, Islamist militants are known to seek shelter in mountainous areas of Jendouba, Kasserine and Kef provinces, near the western border with Algeria. However, terrorist incidents in the Ben Gardane area (Medenine province) in 2016 showed the potential for Islamist extremist activity to also spill over from Libya. Recent developments in that country have also fuelled concerns over the potential return to Tunisia of militants who have fought for IS.</p>\n</p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "summary": "<![CDATA[An increase in the number of cases of Legionnaires' disease has been reported in travellers returning from Dubai. Preliminary investigations indicate that accommodations where the travellers stayed are not the source of infection, and authorities are continuing investigate the source of exposure. People become infected with Legionella when they inhale droplets and mist that come from contaminated bodies of water. The disease does not spread from person to person.           "created": "2016-12-23T15:18:55Z",
          "modified": "2017-01-20T16:23:01Z",
          "categories": [
            {
              "primary": "Medical Alert ",
              "sub": " Disease Outbreak"
            }
          ],
          "long": 55.370676,
          "lat": 24.98742,
          "id": 951646,
          "version": 6,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Dubai </b> - United Arab Emirates (UAE);  <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Legionnaires Disease<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>An increase in the number of cases of Legionnaires' disease has been reported in travellers returning from Dubai. Preliminary investigations indicate that accommodations where the travellers stayed are not the source of infection, and authorities are continuing investigate the source of exposure. People become infected with Legionella when they inhale droplets and mist that come from contaminated bodies of water. The disease does not spread from person to person. A majority of people exposed to the bacteria do not become ill. Those who do fall sick may have a fever, dry cough and difficulty breathing. Seek medical attention if you develop symptoms, especially if you are a smoker, over 50 or have other health conditions. </p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p>Do the following:<br/>\n<ul> <li>Seek medical attention if you develop a fever, flu-like illness or difficulty breathing, especially if you are in a higher risk group.</li> <li>Advise your doctor of your travel history and possible exposure to Legionella.</li>\n</ul>\n<p><br/>\n</p>\n<ul>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Over 30 travel-associated cases of Legionnaires' disease (TALD) have been reported to health authorities in seven EU Member States since 1 October. This is a much higher rate than usually seen in returning travellers and there appears to be a clustering of cases corresponding with travel to Dubai between 10-23 October 2016. This coincides with annual school holidays in a number of EU countries. Most of the cases were 50 years or older and new infections continue to be reported up to the end of December 2016.</p>\n<p>The cases were spread over more than 20 accommodation sites in Dubai, some of which had been implicated in previous years' cases of TALD. Authorities in the UAE have investigated the notified hotels, and report that regular monitoring and additional testing for Legionella bacteria has been negative. A few cases have also been identified in people who were not in hotels but stayed in private homes. Given the widespread geography, it is unlikely that accommodation is the source of exposure. Other locations are likely to be the source although no common place has yet been pinpointed.</p>\n<p>Whether there is also an increase in cases of Legionnaire's disease in the local community is unknown at this stage. </p>\n<p><em>What is Legionnaires' disease? </em></p>\n<p>Legionnaires' disease is caused by Legionella bacteria, which are naturally present in the environment. Some strains are present in soil or potting mix, however, they are most commonly found in water. The bacteria grow best in warm environments such as hot tubs, cooling towers, hot water tanks, large plumbing systems, and air-conditioning systems of buildings. People become infected when they breathe in contaminated water droplets, like steam from a contaminated hot tub. Legionnaires' disease does not spread from person-to-person. It does not spread via drinking water. </p>\n<p>Symptoms usually start within 2-10 days (but can be as long as 19 days) and may include fever, chills, headache, muscle pain, diarrhoea, dry cough and difficulty breathing. Some people only develop a flu-like illness, while others get pneumonia. Most healthy people recover well when treated with antibiotics. Pneumonia caused by Legionella can be serious and is fatal in 5% to 30% of all cases. </p>\n<p>Some people are at a higher risk of infection. Anyone over the age of 50, smokers and former smokers, diabetics, and people with chronic lung conditions (like emphysema or asthma) or other chronic medical conditions like heart and liver disease are more likely to be infected. People who are immunosuppressed, such as those with HIV or on chemotherapy, are also at risk for more serious infections. </p>\n<p>For more information: </p>\n<ul> <li>See the article \"Legionnaires' Disease\" in the Diseases and Prevention dropdown on International SOS Country Guides </li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre</li>\n</ul>\n<p><br/>\n</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Further cases in travellers who have visited Dubai, and those who reside in Dubai may occur. </p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"
