          "created": "2016-11-29T01:00:43Z",
          "modified": "2017-02-28T07:11:47Z",
          "categories": [
            {
              "primary": "Medical Alert ",
              "sub": " Disease Outbreak"
            }
          ],
          "long": -80.551758,
          "lat": 25.958045,
          "id": 951047,
          "version": 11,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Miami-Dade County Florida, Brownsville, Cameron County Texas </b> - United States;  <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Zika virus<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Local transmission of Zika infection remains a risk in Miami-Dade County, Florida and the County of Cameron in the State of Texas. Additionally, a confirmed case is under investigation in the City of McAllen, Hidalgo County, Texas. The CDC has designated Miami-Dade County and Brownsville, Texas, as cautionary “yellow areas” due to the intensity of Zika transmission being unknown and thus a risk to pregnant women. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. Sexual transmission can occur. Although symptoms are usually mild, severe and irreversible birth defects may occur in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider delaying travel to Zika-affected areas. Those who are in affected areas should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission. After travel to the affected areas, ongoing precautions against sexual transmission are recommended.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing.</p>\n<p>For those who will travel to, or are in affected areas:</p>\n<ul> <li>If you are pregnant consult your doctor regarding testing for Zika.</li> <li>Prevent infection by preventing mosquito bites. </li> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535. </li> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks). </li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. </li> <li>Consider using an insecticide-treated bed net.</li> <li>Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room. Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible. </li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission through the use of condoms (or abstinence).</li>\n</ul>\n<p>After travel</p>\n<ul> <li>Pregnant women consult your doctor.</li> <li>Monitor your health for two weeks. If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor. </li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission - Anyone who is pregnant or has a pregnant partner should use condoms (applicable to males and females) or abstain for the duration of the pregnancy. For those whose partners are not pregnant, authorities differ in their recommendations for the duration for which precautions should continue. <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">The World Health Organization</a> advises that everyone should continue precautions for at least 6 months after return. <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">The United States CDC advises</a>: Men should continue precautions for at least 6 months; Women should continue precautions for at least 8 weeks. </li> <li>Consider delaying pregnancy - <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">The World Health Organization</a> recommends couples or women planning a pregnancy, who have returned from an area with Zika transmission, to wait at least 6 months. <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/pregnancy/women-and-their-partners.html\" target=\"_blank\">The United States CDC</a> recommends women who are trying to get pregnant, wait at least 8 weeks and partners of women trying to get pregnant, wait at least 6 months. </li> <li>Continue to prevent mosquito bites for two to three weeks to reduce the risk of infecting local mosquitoes with Zika.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The <a href=\"http://dshs.texas.gov/news/releases/2016/20161209.aspx\" target=\"_blank\">Texas Department of State Health</a> has reported at least six cases of locally acquired mosquito-borne Zika infection in Cameron County in 2016. After the initial case in Brownsville in November, investigations identified several other infected people who lived nearby. The Brownsville area (<a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/zika/intheus/texas-update.html#map\" target=\"_blank\">US CDC map</a>) is now designated a cautionary \"yellow area\", where pregnant woman should consider postponing travel. In February 2017, a case was confirmed in the<a href=\"http://www.mcallen.net/docs/default-source/emergency/zika-case-investigation-in-city-of-mcallen.pdf?sfvrsn=2\" target=\"_blank\"> City of McAllen, Hidalgo County</a>. Further investigations are underway to determine how exposure occurred. So far, no history of travel outside of the county has been established.</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://www.floridahealth.gov/newsroom/2016/12/120916-zika-update.html\" target=\"_blank\">The Florida Department of Health</a> advised that active transmission had ceased in the state on 9 December 2016, over 45 days after the last local mosquito-borne case occurred. Nevertheless, Miami-Dade County is a cautionary area (\"<a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/zika/intheus/florida-update.html\" target=\"_blank\">yellow area</a>\"), with over 250 mosquito transmitted cases between July and December, 2016. The CDC advises pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners, to consider postponing travel to all of Miami-Dade County</p>\n<p>What is Zika?\n</p>\n<p>Zika is a viral disease, mostly transmitted to people by mosquito bites. Zika virus can be passed from an infected pregnant mother to her baby. Spread through sexual contact occurs. Transmission via blood transfusion has occurred. Most people who have Zika infections do not have any symptoms. If symptoms occur, they are similar to chikunguyna or dengue, and include fever, rash, joint and muscle pains or conjunctivitis (red eyes), and typically last up to a week. The virus can cause severe irreversible birth defects, such as \"microcephaly\" (a small head with developmental abnormalities), and serious neurological complications. There is no specific treatment available, and no vaccine to prevent the disease.\n</p>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See the <a href=\"https://pandemic.internationalsos.com/zika\" target=\"_blank\">International SOS Zika website</a>.</li> <li>See \"Zika virus\" in the Diseases and Prevention dropdown on International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p>There is potential for additional Zika cases in the identified areas as well as geographical spread to near-by locations. Public health authorities continue to implement significant measures to prevent widespread transmission.</p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice for managers</b></p><p>If relevant to your employees or travellers, ensure they are aware of:\n</p>\n<ul> <li>Regional health threats including Zika, who is most at risk (such as pregnant women), and prevention strategies. </li> <li>Monitoring the current activity, and any changes to the travel or preventive advice. </li> <li>Who to contact for advice and treatment if they get sick during or after travel. </li> <li>How to access bite prevention resources including insect repellent and bed nets.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Consider: </p>\n<ul> <li>Providing a travel health kit, including insect repellent. </li> <li>Stipulating a medical pre-travel consultation.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/november-2016/zika_tx_fl.png\"/></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>United States; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Mumps<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>A large mumps outbreak continues across the United States. More than 45 states have reported cases, including the District of Columbia. Arkansas, Illinois, Indiana, Iowa, Massachusetts, New York, Oklahoma and Washington State have each recorded more than 100 cases. The outbreaks are largely linked to local university campuses and schools however, workplaces have also been affected in some states. Mumps is caused by a virus that spreads from person-to-person via infected droplets. It is highly contagious and nearly all cases occur among unvaccinated people. Symptoms include fever, painful and swollen glands, pain on swallowing and in men, the testes can become inflamed. Serious complications can occur. All travellers should ensure they are fully vaccinated against mumps.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p> <ul> <li>Ensure you are immune to mumps before travelling. People are immune if they've had the disease before or if they had an adequate series of vaccinations (often given as measles-mumps-rubella, or MMR).</li> <li>Check your vaccination records. Mumps vaccine requires two doses, usually given once to a young child and again before entering school.</li> <li>If you are unsure about your immunity, talk to your doctor.</li> <li>Keep young children and other people who are not immune away from sick people in outbreak areas.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>About individual states: visit the <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/mumps/outbreaks.html\" target=\"_blank\">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention website</a>.</li> <li>About mumps: See \"Mumps\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>About the United States: see the International SOS Country Guide Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Colorado, Illinois, Minnesota, Pennsylvania, South Carolina, Tennessee, Utah, Wisconsin, Iowa </b> - United States;  <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Hantaviruses<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Human cases of hantavirus, linked to rat breeding facilities, have been identified in several states. The outbreak is caused by the Seoul virus, a type of hantavirus. The viruses are carried by infected rodents and are excreted in their saliva, urine and faeces. Humans become infected through inhaling the virus, which is present in rodent excretions, when bitten, or when contaminated particles touch broken skin or enters through the eyes, nose and mouth. Rare cases of the disease, can lead to serious kidney disease. The disease is not generally passed from person to person. Prevention is by avoiding contact with rodents and their environments, and maintaining strict hygiene. The risk to business travellers is low.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p>Do the following:\n<ul> <li>Avoid direct contact with rodents, their faeces, saliva, urine or nests. </li> <li>Pay attention to general hygiene and food storage, always wash your hands thoroughly before eating.</li>\n</ul>\nFor more information:<br/>\n<ul> <li>About hantaviruses: See \"Hantaviruses\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown.</li> <li>See the CDC Seoul Virus outbreak <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/hantavirus/outbreaks/seoul-virus/index.html\" target=\"_blank\">website</a> </li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Confirmed human cases of Seoul hantavirus have been reported in at least nine US states but pet rats have been shipped to a number of other states for onward sale to pet owners. These are labelled as being under investigation. As of 23 February, the CDC lists the following breakdown of confirmed and investigation states:</p>\n<p>There have been 16 laboratory-confirmed recent human cases of Seoul virus, with positive tests in either humans or rats in the following states Colorado, Illinois, Iowa, Minnesota, Philadelphia, South Carolina, Tennessee, Utah, and Wisconsin.</p>\n<p>There are 18 states with facilities currently under investigation in: Alabama, Colorado, Delaware, Georgia, Illinois, Iowa, Louisiana, Maryland, Michigan, Minnesota, Missouri, North Dakota, New Jersey, Philadelphia, South Carolina, Tennessee, Utah, and Wisconsin.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/february-2017/us_seoul_24feb2017.png\"/></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>United States; </b> <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Entry restrictions<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>President Donald Trump on 6 March signed an Executive Order involving new immigration procedures, including a 90-day restriction on citizens of Iran, Libya, Somalia, Sudan, Syria and Yemen from applying for visas to travel to the US. The measure will take effect on <strong>16 March</strong>. Citizens of these countries with valid US visas, those who already hold legal permanent residence in the US (green card), and dual nationals travelling on a passport from a non-restricted country will not be affected. The new Executive Order also bars refugees from entering the US for 120 days and caps the number of refugees admitted at 50,000 per year. </p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Travellers from the affected countries should contact the US embassy/relevant diplomatic missions for more information on the temporary restrictions.</li> <li>Nationals from countries not listed above are currently not affected by the Executive Order; however, the Order states that further countries may be added to the list of those affected by restrictions in the future. Monitor relevant developments in the coming weeks and months. </li> <li><strong>We do not hold information on specific flights</strong>. Should related demonstrations occur at airports in the short-term, we do not expect they will affect flight schedules. However, in the case of protests, travellers should contact the relevant airline, travel agent or the airport's website directly to reconfirm the status of flights prior to setting out.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on the US for further developments.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>The new Executive Order has been drafted in response to a temporary judicial stay on a similar measure issued on 27 January, which included a 90-day restriction on the entry of Iraqi, Iranian, Libyan, Somali, Sudanese, Syrian and Yemeni nationals to the US, as well as the prohibition of all refugees from entering the country for 120 days. The new Executive Order recalls the previous order entirely and replaces it. </p>\n<p>The primary differences between the previous Order and the new one include the removal of Iraq from the list of countries explicitly affected by travel restrictions, and the exemption of individuals who already possess valid visas as of 16 March for travel to the US from restrictions. The Order was originally expected to be signed on 1 March, but the signing was delayed for undisclosed reasons. All travellers should expect increased scrutiny while passing through customs and immigration, including the possibility of being asked to undergo additional screening measures on a case-by-case basis.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>As with the previous Executive Order, legal challenges are likely to emerge in the coming weeks and months, though a definitive adjudication on the measure is not likely in the short term. </p>\n<p>Precedent suggests protests are possible in the coming days and weeks following the signing of the Order in major cities, including along key thoroughfares, in main plazas and in front of government buildings. Precedent also suggests protests may take place at airports throughout the country over the restrictions, though these are likely to be less pronounced and widespread, should they occur, as similar demonstrations which followed the previous Order.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

Five out of the six provinces have reported cases, with Shefa being the most affected. Dengue is consistently present in the country. The disease is spread by mosquitoes, and is present in both rural and urban areas. Dengue can cause a range of symptoms and has no particular treatment. Some people, especially those who have been infected before, get a more severe form that can lead to fatal complications. Prevent infection by avoiding mosquito bites.\n</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<ul> <li>When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks).</li> <li>Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535.</li> <li>Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire.           "created": "2017-03-07T03:53:57Z",
          "modified": "2017-03-07T03:53:57Z",
          "categories": [
            {
              "primary": "Medical Alert ",
              "sub": " Disease Outbreak"
            }
          ],
          "long": 108.339537,
          "lat": 14.315424,
          "id": 953332,
          "version": 1,
          "specialadvisory": false,
          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Advisory <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Vietnam </b> - Vietnam;  <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Zika virus<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Zika outbreak is ongoing in parts of Vietnam, particularly in Ho Chi Minh city. Zika is transmitted primarily through mosquito bites. Sexual transmission occurs. Although symptoms of Zika are usually mild and self-limited, there is a risk of severe and irreversible birth defects in the babies of women infected when pregnant. Pregnant women are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing. Those who are in affected areas should protect themselves against mosquito bites and sexual transmission. After travel, ongoing precautions against sexual transmission are recommended.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p>Pregnant women, women trying to conceive and their partners are advised to discuss their risks with their doctor and consider postponing travel to any area where Zika virus transmission is ongoing. In addition to the infectious risks of any destination, it is important to consider the standard of available healthcare, and the availability of obstetric and neonatal specialist support (should it be required). Pregnant women - discuss your risks with your doctor, including whether you should postpone travel.\n<br/>\n<br/>\nFor those who will travel to, or are in Vietnam:\n<br/>\n<ul> <li>Prevent infection by preventing mosquito bites: Use an effective insect repellent that contains DEET, Picaridin, PMD, or IR3535. When outdoors, wear clothing that covers most of your body (long sleeves, long pants, socks). Ensure windows are covered with fly-wire. Consider using an insecticide-treated bed net. Use \"knock-down\" insect spray to kill mosquitoes in your room. Choose air conditioned accommodation if possible.</li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission through the use of condoms (or abstinence).</li>\n</ul>\n<p>After travel:\n</p>\n<ul> <li>Pregnant women - Consult your doctor. You may be advised to be tested for Zika whether or not you develop symptoms. Your doctor may also arrange additional monitoring or specialist care. </li> <li>All travellers - Monitor your health for two weeks. </li> <li>If you develop symptoms, ensure that you see a doctor and advise them of your travel history. This is especially important if you are, or are trying to fall pregnant.</li> <li>Prevent sexual transmission - Anyone who has a pregnant partner should use condoms (applicable to males and females) or abstain for the duration of the pregnancy. For those whose partners are not pregnant, authorities differ in their recommendations for the duration for which precautions should continue. The <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">World Health Organization</a> advises that everyone should continue precautions for at least 6 months after return. The <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/prevention/protect-yourself-during-sex.html\" target=\"_blank\">United States CDC</a> advises: Men should continue precautions for at least 6 months; Women should continue precautions for at least 8 weeks. </li> <li>Consider delaying pregnancy - The <a href=\"http://www.who.int/csr/resources/publications/zika/sexual-transmission-prevention/en/\" target=\"_blank\">World Health Organization</a> recommends couples or women planning a pregnancy, who have returned from an area with Zika transmission, to wait at least 6 months. The <a href=\"http://www.cdc.gov/zika/pregnancy/women-and-their-partners.html\" target=\"_blank\">United States CDC</a> recommend women trying to get pregnant wait at least 8 weeks and partners of women trying to get pregnant wait at least 6 months. </li> <li>Prevent transmission to local mosquitoes. If you are returning to an area that has mosquitoes which can transmit Zika, continue to prevent mosquito bites for 2 to 3 weeks (e.g. using insect repellent). This will reduce the risk of infecting local mosquitoes with Zika, and therefore reduce the risk of an outbreak.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Since 2016, there have been over 200 cases of locally-acquired Zika infection nationwide affecting at least 11 cities and provinces. The majority of these are from Ho Chi Minh city. At least 20 out of 24 districts in the city have reported cases. Sporadic cases have been detected in travellers visiting Vietnam.\nZika may have been established in the local mosquito population in parts of the Vietnam, however the extent and intensity of spread is uncertain at this time.\n<br/>\n<br/>\nWhat is Zika?\n<br/>\n<br/>\nZika is a viral disease, mostly transmitted to people by mosquito bites. Zika virus can be passed from an infected pregnant mother to her baby. Spread through sexual contact occurs. Transmission via blood transfusion has been reported. Most people who have Zika infections do not have any symptoms. If symptoms occur, they are similar to chikungunya or dengue, and include fever, rash, joint and muscle pains or conjunctivitis (red eyes), and typically last up to a week. The virus can cause severe irreversible birth defects, such as \"microcephaly\" (a small head with developmental abnormalities), and serious neurological complications. There is no specific treatment available, and no vaccine to prevent the disease.\n<br/>\n<br/>\nFor more information:\n</p>\n<ul> <li>See the <a href=\"https://pandemic.internationalsos.com/zika\" target=\"_blank\">International SOS Zika website</a>.</li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide on Vietnam.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>The situation is continuing to evolve. The transmission intensity is uncertain. An increase in cases is likely as screening and testing is increased.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

The firm in September 2016 promised to pay the authorities for the environmental damage caused, but fishermen and local communities in affected areas have continued to demand compensation and and the closure of the plant. Gatherings are liable to take place, particularly on Sundays, in locations worst affected by the pollution, including Ha Tinh, Nghe An, Quang Binh, Quang Tri and Thua Thien-Hue provinces.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><ul> <li>Avoid all related protests to mitigate the risk of incidental exposure to violence. Liaise with local contacts and ensure that there is no rally taking place in the vicinity before visiting facilities or locations that may be mistaken as being Chinese and Taiwanese (for example, as a result of corporate branding).</li> <li>All ethnic-Chinese travellers should keep a low profile as a routine precaution. Those who are not Chinese or Taiwanese nationals (for example, ethnic-Chinese Singaporean) should be aware of their profile and take steps to denote their nationality, such as carrying passport identification. Chinese and Taiwanese nationals should avoid overt displays of nationality.</li> <li>If inadvertently caught up in a demonstration or if protesters and/or the security forces begin to gather, quickly but calmly leave the area and relocate to a secure location such as your local office or accommodation. Do not stop to watch or photograph rallies.</li> <li>Monitor our alerts on Vietnam for further updates.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>Protests over the alleged pollution began in April 2016 in the country's major cities, particularly over the weekends. Demonstrations in major cities are likely remain small and peaceful, with participants likely to engage in picketing, sit-ins and - at most - minor acts of vandalism such as throwing paint at diplomatic missions. Nonetheless, scuffles between the police and demonstrators have occurred during previous events, with the former using batons and checkpoints and security cordons to control the unrest.</p>\n<p>In recent months, protesters have rallied in Ha Tinh, Nghe An and Quang Binh. Most recently on 14 February, hundreds of protesters from Quynh Luu district (Nghe An province) marched to People's Court in Ky Anh (Ha Tinh province) to file a petition, but were stopped on the way; scuffles between protesters and police were reported. On 5 February, several hundred residents protested at the local government offices in the Quang Loc commune of Ba Don town (Quang Binh). Prior to this, hundreds of demonstrators blocked parts of National Highway 1A on 14 and 23 January, resulting in congestion.</p>\n<p>The majority of protests have so far occurred in Ha Tinh, with activists belonging to a local Roman Catholic diocese heeding calls from priests to march peacefully. The Catholic community in Vietnam has periodically held demonstrations over environmental or land acquisition grievances. Nonetheless, such demonstrations do not pose a serious challenge to broader social or governmental stability.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Assessment</b></p><p>Although there have been attempts to equate the current protests with a bout of social unrest in May 2014 that targeted Chinese nationals and interests, as well as individuals and interests perceived to be of Chinese origin, we do not assess this to be the case. Those incidents occurred during a period of particularly acute diplomatic tension between Vietnam and China.</p>\n<p>Since the May 2014 demonstrations, which quickly escalated, Vietnam has taken significant steps to prevent a repeat of such incidents, such as providing physical security to Chinese-owned facilities deemed vulnerable. Chinese nationals and interests, as well as individuals who may be mistaken for Chinese nationals, should nonetheless be aware that their profile makes them vulnerable to low-level verbal harassment. However, physical assaults of such individuals should remain rare.</p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"

In February, there appears to be a decline in the number of new cases in most areas. The majority of activity is in the governorates of Al Hudayday, Dhale, Hajjah and Ta'izz. Cholera is consistently present in the country and is spread via contaminated food and water. Symptoms include vomiting and profuse, watery diarrhoea which can lead to severe dehydration. Travellers staying in quality accommodation are at low risk. Pay strict attention to hygiene and consider vaccination.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<ul> <li>If available, vaccination is recommended for travellers and relief workers who are likely to encounter unsanitary conditions, or will have limited access to safe water.</li> <li>Drink only boiled or bottled water, water that has been treated with chlorine or iodine, or carbonated beverages.</li> <li>Select food, water and other beverages carefully.</li> <li>Choose food that has been thoroughly cooked while fresh and is served hot.</li> <li>Avoid ice, raw fruit, and raw vegetables (including salad). Fruit and vegetables that you peel yourself are safer choices.</li> <li>Pay close attention to hygiene. Wash your hands frequently, especially before eating.</li> <li>Get medical advice / treatment if you develop severe diarrhoea and/or vomiting.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Consider:</p> <ul> <li>Carrying water purification supplies with you.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p><p><b>More detail</b></p><p>For more information:</p>\n<ul> <li>See \"Cholera\" in the \"Diseases and Prevention\" dropdown on International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>See the Travel Advice article \"Selecting Safe Food and Water\" on the International SOS Country Guides.</li> <li>About Yemen: See the International SOS Country Guide.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.</li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://www.internationalsos.com//~/media/dynamic-content/alert-maps/november-2016/yemen-cholera.png\"/></p>]]>"

          "body": "<![CDATA[<p><b><i>Level</i></b>: Notice <br />\n<b><i>Location</i></b>: <b>Harare </b> - Zimbabwe;  <br />\n<b><i>Category</i></b>: Typhoid fever<br />\n</p>\n<p><p>Typhoid cases continue to be reported in Harare, particularly in the Mbare district. Typhoid outbreaks are common in Zimbabwe including regions in and around Harare. Typhoid is a serious bacterial infection spread through contaminated food or water. Symptoms include fever, headache, loss of appetite, abdominal pain, diarrhoea or constipation, and rash. The infection is treated with antibiotics. Prevention is through careful selection of food and water, and vaccination.</p></p>\n\n<p><p><b>Advice</b></p><p>Do the following:</p>\n<ul> <li>Consider vaccination (generally recommended for all travellers prior to departure).</li> <li>Maintain a high level of personal hygiene; wash hands frequently with soap and water.</li> <li>Drink only boiled or bottled water, water that has been treated with chlorine or iodine, or carbonated beverages.</li> <li>Avoid ice.</li> <li>Select safe food. Meals should be thoroughly cooked and served hot. Avoid under-cooked or raw meat, fish or shellfish, raw fruit and vegetables.</li>\n</ul>\nFor more information:<br/>\n<ul> <li>See \"Typhoid\" in the Diseases and Prevention dropdown and the Travel Advice Article \"Selecting Safe Food and Water\" on the International SOS Country Guides. </li> <li>See the International SOS Country Guide for Zimbabwe.</li> <li>Contact any International SOS assistance centre.<br/> <ul> </ul> </li>\n</ul></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>\n\n<p></p>]]>"
